RATING: 8
[First Impressions]
It really seems to be the command & colors system at the top of its form. Still light and easy to play, but with a couple of new elements (namely leaders, evasion maneuvers and the interesting characterization of units) that bring to the system the right amount of strategy and tactics. I need to play it more to confirm the vote, but I think it’s a really good design so far.

I like the wooden blocks. Illustrations are not exactly stunning, the board and the cards are of... well... GMT quality, but there’s nothing to really complain about: everything works just fine.

RATING: 7
After not being too enthused by C&C for all these years, I finally get it, I realized I don't enjoy complex war-games as much as I used to and with much less time on my hands these days complex games rarely if ever get to the table. So C&C fills a niche slot - a low complexity , streamlined and enjoyable light war-game. 
Ancients is more fun to play than other games in the series because of the lack of ranged units which abound in other titles like Battle Cry or Napoleonics. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Probably my favourite of the Commands & Colo(u)rs series.

Pros:
I love the Ancients era, particularly Ancient Roman and Greek periods. The C&C system adds an element of fog of war/chaos. The wooden blocks look great. Set up is not as long as other C&C games as most battles have very little terrain. I like the emphasis on holding the line. Very thematic. Fun!

Cons:
Set up still takes a while as you have to dig out specific units for specific scenarios. The terrain tiles look cheap. There is no "facing" for the units which means that (other than blocking retreats) it makes no difference if a unit is attacked from the front or from behind. It is rare that the historical victor doesn't win the battle, which may not be fun for the person playing the losing side.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
Great simplified gaming system. This series is played often and meant to be played fast. It's not that difficult... 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
reviewed on my blog.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Already I must agree that this is the best C&C game yet (though I've yet to try Napoleonics). With the very sober rules system small differences in units stats play out in vastly different ways, and it's striking different they feel in practice. For example, auxiliary light infantry and basic light infantry at first glance seem very similar, but after a couple turns you'll notice that auxiliary act as light shock infantry while basic light infantry are simply ranged harassers.

The game is still a bit heavy on the dice, and I think a combat results table would bring the game to a new level. Still, for a breezy war game (a rare creature) this is a treat.

RATING: 10
This was one of those games that you play and wonder where it has been all of your life.  Right before BGGCon 2013 I played an epic scenario with some friends from the local game group.  At this point I have gotten pretty jaded overall when it comes to new games but this one was an instant hit.

At BGGCon I ended up playing a dozen or more scenarios and a friend and I ended up stickering 2 expansions just so we could get a chance to play the cool Spartan scenarios.  I ended up buying a complete set from a guy selling his and it was in excellent condition and saved me a lot of stickering.  I am really looking forward to trying out all of the scenarios in this game and getting to explore this deceptively simple game system.

I am still at the point where I am learning something new every game, which is exciting for me.  I am however looking forward to the point where I have all of the minor rules down solid and I can run Epic Scenarios for friends.

If you are on the fence do not hesitate, this is a great game and very worthy of getting in to, even if you just go for the base game.

RATING: 5
Simple, fast-playing, but very abstract game with some strange rules (heavy units can be killed just as easily as light ones, attempting to cross a river actually makes you safer when attacked, etc.) The more varied units in this game compared to Memoir 44 makes it a little bit more tactically interesting. But not by much. I know little of how battles from this period was fought, but I suspect this game does a bad job at simulating them.

RATING: 9.5
Quick to set up, puts you immediately into tactician mode, takes enough out of your hands ensuring the best laid plans will often go astray (highly contemporary to the era), no stacking, no need for informational markers to clutter play...

The best solitaire war gaming experience I have ever had without adjusting a single rule or procedure. This game forces you to do the best with what you have, not necessarily where you want to do it and in the face of a reactionary defender. Extreme solitaire compatibility as a result.

RATING: 5
A little "heavier" than Battle Cry, but still a very good game.  I find myself becoming more interested in this period because of the game.

Lowered the rating from 7 to 5.  Scenarios are almost always unbalanced to the historical winner. I found that after playing some of the Worthington Games offerings which use a die for activation, the cards plus the right-center-left battlefield limits the types of battles that can be portrayed. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
More detail and strategy than the other C&C-based (Memoir, Battle Cry, etc) base games.  But there is enough C&C that if you have played one, it's easy to catch on.

Very good because you really have to think through your moves more.  Not as much "gamey" moves as other C&C-based games.  You focus more on your formations and playing the cards in your hands in the optimal order to carry out your plans.

RATING: 7
A fun, light system, and a great way to introduce the uninitiated to wargaming. That said, the more you play, the more you discover C&C:A has far more strategic depth than simply moving up your line, crashing into your opponent, and throwing some dice. It’s not a game I’d want to play for entire night all too often, but when I’m short on time, or need a gap-filler, it’s great to know a game this fun with true challenges can be played in less than an hour. If you can find enough of the expansions to fill out the necessary blocks, the Epic games are a blast.

RATING: 7
Ultimately, I'd like to own a block wargame. I enjoy the tactile aspect and particularly like when the block faces are hidden from an opponent, to represent the Fog Of War. 

C+C:A is a sound game. Sound isn't good enough in my small collection. C+C:A isn't enough of a departure from Chess - likely the FIRST game in my collection. The hand management and unit variation features are pleasant, but not failed to add the necessary drama I might enjoy to shift the dynamic enough from the serious, high-stakes consequences of decisions in Chess to the more fun, front-loaded gambling of conflict-heavy games like Flash Duel or Cry Havoc. I would even look to Claustrophobia before this.

RATING: 7

Rating is more "for us" (ok, then it's maybe a 6, 7 tops) than actual evaluation of the game quality.

Note:  compared to BattleLore, the command cards are much more effective, and less frustrating, and the "keep your men together" is more subtle and interesting.

RATING: 9.5
Now, this is a great game, but it isn't for everyone. For instance, advanced syphilitics may find they lack the motor control necessary to manipulate the game pieces, and the comatose may find it difficult to get into the feel of the game. I've also heard (but cannot confirm from firsthand experience) that zombies have difficulty focusing on the higher tactical  decisions in the games and may wander off in search of brains. But if your spinal column hasn't been devoured by spirochetes, you're conscious, and you're not one of the living dead, cursed to walk the streets in eternal torment as punishment for a life of sin, you'll probably enjoy this game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I like the order system and the blocks are okay. I would have preferred counters or rotating step loss blocks and maybe separate decks. The blocks as they are feel precisely like the weird miniatures replacement compromise that they were included for.

I really dislike the way the dice system interacts with the VP system. You play to a very small number of victory points, but the game doesn't value them properly. There are 1/36 chances to eliminate stuff like light cav and leaders outright, and you are awarded VP for it. It might be realistic, but it pretty much ruins the game for me. Killing a light cav on turn two with ranged units on a low probability shot could be 25% of the necessary VP. It's too random to be enjoyable.

RATING: 8
spanning from Rome Vs. Carthage and Alexander the Greats campaigns. It is still a wonderful game and for me personally I have found the battles more tense and exciting than those in the other games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
The 3rd game in the Command & Colors series behind Battle Cry and Memoir '44. Probably a better game than either of these two. Better than Battlelore also. It still has dice and cards so the luck is a bit on the heavy side but the cards are more diverse in this game and the rules give the players more meaningful choices. You also get more different types of units.

For those thinking that plastic minis would be better for a game like this I think I've come to actually prefer the blocks. The blocks are easier to handle and easier to tell apart in my opinion. Imagine 13 different types of units including 5 different infantry units and 3 different calvalry units each of which look similar. The blocks are also easier to store.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
C&C:A is my 1st foray into war games. I researched and selected it very carefully, so it's not surprising that I quite like the game. Overall it is much easier to learn to play then I expected. Though beware of the first play when all the stickers need to be applied (seriously an hour+) during initial setup. Once the rules/system are understood the game is fast and tense.   

It is worth noting that most of the scenarios are NOT balanced, so any competition should be based on playing the scenario twice (switching sides) and summing the margins of victory/defeat.

Overall a fantastic medium-light wargame that should be easy to enjoy for the majority of strategy gamers.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Yahtzee with colored dice. Well, I don't dislike playing this game, but I don't consider this as a wargame, but rather a strategy game. But it's a bit of fun too, in spite of the widespread combat results. The (un)lucky die rolls can be frustrating.

The rules, card deck, blocks, leaders and scenarios are too generic. You'll never get a Marathon, Zama, Cynocyphalae, etc. feeling. Well at least I didn't. There are no unit facing, scenario specific cards and I have to look on the unit sheet frequently, because I can't memorize, while the needed information could have been on the blocks. In its' essence the game is not much different from Battle Cry, Memoir '44 and Battlelore, though it has some improvements.

Two other points of critism is that although units take hits they don't decrease in strength until eliminated and that battles suddenly ends - when the required number of units have been eliminated. There is no anti-climax. Just a sudden end, which always feels dissatisfying to me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Great gateway wargame. It uses the famous game system with the cards and the board divided in sections found in many games like Memoir 44, BattleCry BattleLore... In Ancients the addition of leaders take the gameplay to a higher level. The game mechanics remain simple but they provide a lot of interesting choices. Each battle takes about an hour. It is truly an enjoyable game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Very similar in its mechanics to Memoir 44, this game is perhaps a little bit more fun for euro gamers who, perhaps ironically, might prefer battling with "ancients" rather than army men, and blocks rather than small plastic figurines. 

On the other hand, the stickered blocks for the "light units" in this game are crazy small such that it is very hard to figure out which is which for first time gamers.  Hmmm...  

This is a nice, basic war game driven by card play, unit capability determinations, spatial planning, attack timing considerations, and the rolling of specialized dice that can offer greater hits when a leader is involved, and certain rolls that force the enemy to retreat.  You have two basic kinds of attack: ranged and close combat.

For first timers, the rules are quite fiddly since certain units can and cannot do various cool things, and none of these capabilities/restrictions are shown on the actual units.  You need to reference the elaborate, but well designed, player aides.  Like Memoir 44, you can play different scenarios with varied win conditions, ensuring replayability.

RATING: 9

I recommend purchasing just the EPIC expansion and the extra board.

Richard Borg's best game, in my opinion.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Nice, simple depiction of ancient battles. Easily my favourite of the commands&colors games, I much prefer the blocks over plastic figures.

The rules are pretty straightforward, and there is a definite hand management side to the game besides the actual battle tactics, but I think that once you learn to play well it gives a pretty good, simplified abstraction of warfare in this era. It captures the essential differences in the weapons systems and unit types, the importance of cohesion (both at unit and army level), leadership, manoeuvre and light unit screening. I think the system only has two flaws, in that it doesn't have much difference in staying power between different unit types, and that due to the nature of card play extensive manoeuvre on the battlefield is hard to accomplish. In fact, I'm trying to learn the much more complex SPQR system now, and I can definitely see a lot of parallels between the mechanics of the two game systems.

Considering each battle only takes 30 to 60 minutes to play, and how enjoyable it is, this is one of my favourite light wargames.

RATING: 7
Diversity of troops in this game creates an extremely rewarding system that will likely outshine Memoir 44 and Battle Cry. The flaw in the game is the poor components design (board, blocks, dice). It is extremely difficult to tell different units apart at a quick glance (particularly for the small block, light infantry troops). While the artwork adds some character to the game, it's certainly not user-friendly. I'd rather make my own board and buy some small miniatures than play with the components that come in the GMT box. While I would rate the game highly, there's no way this game is worth the retail price. Can't imagine spending even more money on the expansions...

RATING: 9
A Memoir'44 és a Battlelore után sikerült kipróbálnom végre ezt is! A játékrendszer ugyanarra az alapra épül mindhárom esetben. A Battlelore magasra tette a mércét, a C & C pedig még kicsit talán a Battlelore-on is túltesz. Bár nem egyszerű összevetni a kettőt, a sok hasonlóság ellenére sem (ezért is kaptak azonos pontot egyelőre). Papíron a Battlelore a nehezebb, de másban rejlik a nehézsége: ott a tudáskártyák révén egy másfajta dimenzió is kerül a játékba bonyolítás képpen. Itt, a Command & Colorsban sokkal kevesebb a kellék, de rengeteg (tucatnyinál is több) féle egység van, ez is a játék egyik hátránya, hogy 4 oldalnyi segédlet kell a különböző egységek képességeinek áttekintéséhez (és ez még csak az alapjáték): melyik egység mennyi kockával dobhat közelharcban, képes-e távolsági harcra, mennyit léphet, milyen kockadobásokkal ér el találatot, stb. Úgy érzem, néhány csata lejátszása mindenképp szükséges az egységek megismeréséhez, de a '44 után most is jó kort választott a szerző, a római-pun háborúkkal szívesen elszüttyögök, és a témaválasztás tekintetében mindenképp jobban tetszik ez a játék, mint a Battlelore.
A legtöbb taktikai lehetőséget egyébként az elefántok és a vezetők kínálják, de a kétféle harcmodor is sok dilemmát kínál, színesítve a játékot.
A játékidő nem vészes. A kártyák nyelvfüggőek, muszáj volt játék előtt magyarítani őket.
Ahogy a Battlelore-nál, úgy itt is eljátszották azt, hogy tele van a szabály kiegekre utalással, és majd később megjelenő egységek képeivel, ez eléggé zavaró.
A játék kivitele szép, végre nem műanyag, hanem fa bábukkal küzdhetünk, de ez is egy olyan darab, amit 1 csata kedvéért nem éri meg elővenni, olyan sokáig tart elpakolni (persze attól is függ, ki hogyan tárolja)...
Összességében mindenképp ajánlom annak, aki szereti a római kori történelmet, és nem riad vissza az alapvetően egyszerű, de bizonyos részletekben aprólékosan elmerülő szabálytól. A segédletek egyébként elég profik, kb. a szabály 80%-át magukba sűrítik. 
Záró megjegyzésként annyit, hogy nem értem, miért nem favorizálják ezt a játékot közel sem annyira, mint a Memoirt, holott szerintem nyújt ez is akkora élményt. Gondolom a világháború, mint téma, többeket érdekel. De kidolgozottság tekintetében ég és föld a két játék. Tehát aki a Memoirt szereti, annak ezt is nagyon ajánlom (max. fél órányi plusz infó szükséges ehhez a játékhoz)!

In my view the C&C system didn't succeed in the Civil War or the basic WW2 game (only after 10 expansions or so of Mem44 it got the much needed diversity).
But here the system excels ! Troops are perfectly portrayed with enough variety and diversity to make it highly interesting within a rather "easy to get into" system.
I do like the Napoleonic and The Great War variant too, but the C&C Ancients system is simply its best incarnation to date.

Recommended for both starter and intermediate wargamer. Plays fast too.
It is simply the best period for this C&C system.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[i]Posted: 4/5/11, Rated: 9[/i]

At this point I have to say that I'm a fan of [person=25]Richard Borg's[/person] "Commands" mechanic.  I have copies of [i][thing=10630]Memoir '44[/thing][/i] and [i][thing=25417]BattleLore[/thing][/i], yet I still wanted to try this title, too.  Am I ever glad that I did!

While I love the production value that [company=1027]Days of Wonder[/company] poured into the other two titles (now [company=17]Fantasy Flight[/company] for the latter), they have always felt frivolous with their miniatures.  That's not a bad thing as games are meant to be fun, after all.  Still, when I finally emerged from the wracks of sticker torture, I was rewarded with a game whose appearance resonates with me.  The look of blocks on a muted board makes me think of the sand tables used in military classes.  The look is admittedly simplistic, which fits this time period perfectly.

Mechanics-wise, [i]C&C:A[/i] feels very familiar.  We're still learning how to move a large host across an open field: should we be methodical, maintaining support even though we're taking opportunity fire from archers?  Or should we rush headlong into battle, forsaking support to close with our enemy?  The learning curve here is a challenge.

But the one thing that I truly enjoy about this game is logging the game play afterwards.  By taking the pile of discarded orders, you can effectively recreate the battle.  The titles of the cards themselves provide the narrative.  It's a metagame aspect to be sure, but [i]Commands & Colors: Ancients[/i] truly delivers on multiple fronts.

RATING: 9
I always liked the idea of the C&C series.  The previous games in this series were more 7s for me: Battle Cry, because while it was good, there wasn't any extra support beyond the base set; Memoir '44, because of the theme.

However, C&C: Ancients has the variety and the support and a theme I like, making it pretty much the perfect game in this series for me.  I like the extra mechanics given, and didn't realize how much I'd missed the leaders in Battle Cry.  This is an incredibly approachable yet complex enough wargame, and I can't wait to see what's in the expansions coming up (the first one includes a whopping 20 scenarios and 300 more units).

If there were ever a C&C: Fantasy, that would most likely hit a rating of 10 for me.

I wonder if we can somehow get a clandestine view of Richard Borg's C&C stove....

RATING: N/A
Good game.  I was slow to play at the start of my first game and then just started moving and the game came together.  I had little experience with a game like this coming in.  Fortunately, I was put on the side with the advantage the first time which helped.  There was no terrain and not a ton of variety in the pieces in play, but the game was still interesting.  I can see how the complexity of choices will grow with other aspects in the game.

Second scenario was good with a bit of terrain (lake and hills) and played in less than an hour.  For me, the system is easy to grasp.  There are a few details, such as advance and attack with certain units, that I miss here and there but I expect that would get better with more plays.  Also, I've played the side with the command edge twice now; I imagine the level of difficulty jumps when your command level is 4 instead of 6.  It always helps to sling a rock at the leader and take him out on the first turn of the game.  :)

I've rated this game an 8 and see that it could definitely go up with more plays.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I am not a big ancients battles game fan; I played a fair amount of the old SPI Spartan game, which was adequate if largely colorless; since then, I have tried and moved on from a dozen different battles and systems, none of which have grabbed me.

After buying a few GBOH games in 2003, and laboring through the rules, and scratching my head in disbelief over how populer this series is vs. the absolutely mastubatory rules complexity, I sold all of them, and wondered if someone would ever come out with a Lite Ancients series that didn't stray into moderate (as several others I've played seem to be prone to), and could cover enough ancients ground to keep the learning curve reasonable through the whole ancients time frame.  

Then along came this series, and while some may argue it's heavy Euro, it stays just to the correct side of Lite to me, and I embrace it as a "M44 Ancients" lite wargame.  The different sized 3D blocks give a nice look, and impart size differences to the combat arms involved; the sticker icons are clear and colorful.  The rules offer plenty of combat arms differentiation, and I've set up and played every game in under 2 hours.

This series gives me a huge payback for the time invested, and is perfectly suited to my level of interest in this period's battles.

RATING: 5
People said this was better than other games using the same system, but they were wrong.  The whole movement by flanks thing making no sense and often causing all kinds of problems where players may not be fighting each other and instead are fighting their cards.  CCA addresses this by merely adding more cards that let you move in multiple areas.  You would think after how many games in this line that the designer might have a better idea, but no.  The real problem is that on top of this arbitrary and light combat system, Borg added a ridiculous amount of chrome and fiddliness.  All of these unit types add nothing to the gameplay, the retreat rules are onerous but seem no better than the ones in Battlecry and the list goes on.

Finally the setup times are ridiculous and putting all the stickers on the blocks was painful.  This was an absolutely atrocious design decision.  I would say I am not going to support GMT any more, but that is already a given considering that on top of their production they also can't make a good game.

I am done with this system and I am done with the one-trick pony that is Borg.

RATING: 10
Of the three Richard Borg "BattleCry"-style boardgamesin print, Commands and Colors Ancients is the example that hews closest to its theme and rewards the more thoughtful player. Also, the game is a few steps more complex than its siblings but does not sacrifice the ease with which the rules are learned nor the quick pace at which the game plays. The Command Card deck is well balanced and the sheer variety of unit types and terrain to play them on will keep players busy learning their unique traits in games for quite a while. And the production is excellent. While not as lavish as the DOW games, this item was still "done right" where it counts: in the rules and the fun in playing. If you enjoy Memoir: 44 and BattleLore, you really must try this. Prepare yourself for a remarkably different game!

RATING: 6.5
The dice can be frustrating, but you have to keep in mind that in this game you control mainly the organization of your troops. A match between evenly-skilled players could end up being decided by the dice, although you can say that about any game in which they play a significant role.

I still really like this game. I really feel like a general in an ancient battle. First I thought that the history theme was just some flavor thrown in, but after a while I found that it's truly important for the overall game experience.

I like the square pieces better than the (gray) battlelore miniatures (easier to hold when moving troops); I hate how brown and beige the game is, though, and they could've printed the map on something less flimsy.

RATING: 10
Very cool system! Its nice to be able to play a full battle in an hour. THis means the game will be played much more often than longer games.

Without a lot of rules it manages to capture a lot of the "feel" of combat in the ancients period.
Most scenarios are unbalanced though and that is a drawback to me.
I know historical battles are rarely balanced but it would have been easy during the playtesting phase to determine a set of optionals for each battle that would make it a balanced contest (for example have one side start with 1 or more victory banners, or increase/decrease one or both players hand of command cards.

I have now played 100(!!!) games of CC:A and its expansions and I love it just as much if not even more than when I started

RATING: 8

Initial Impressions from limited playings: I enjoyed Battle Cry as a novel approach to tactical recreations in the beer & pretzels category, but found the card/sector interaction constraining. Here, in C&C:Ancients, that constraint elevates to outright annoyance, because the underlying game itself was outstanding! There were other design decisions that also detracted from the experience I had.

I was put off by the absence of figs in a game that seemed clearly intended to showcase miniatures, but was prepared to consider the advantages of the block approach so successfully employed in the Columbia Games series. The next disappointment then, was to discover that this is basically a traditional hex and counter war game but with unusually thick and durable counters, counters that you have to assemble yourself by fixing endless decals onto pieces of wood.

Further, not only are there no "stands" to aid in unit placement/organization, far too many of the smaller blocks are almost impossible to distinguish from each other except for a tiny icon that needed a magnifying glass to discern. Getting a scenario ready to play turned out to be a major chore.

Finally, after ages of sorting and arranging we were ready to play and looking forward to the unit manoeuvre and interaction that is the real draw in ancients combat, only to find that the cards made it difficult to do anything sensible or coherent. The game stresses unit cohesion and mutual support so pushing units willy nilly or choosing to do nothing are often the "Hobbes' choices" you get to make. The one saving grace is that some of the cards give you an option to activate a unit of your choice in a given sector if you do not have the specific type of unit specified on the card; however, it was only mildly successful in mitigating the frustration I was feeling.

Overall, I was quite disappointed.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Similar system to Battle Cry & Memoir'44 but different enough to consider owning. 
Probably the best in the series, but I'm biased liking this period of history. I think the system best suits this period and the changes I like - battling back, terrain etc. I also think the 'damaged units still attack the same' works in this period, consider the damage as morale loss - get to zero blocks and the unit breaks ... there's also the rule that warbands are less effective when they take their first hit. 

Only moans so far are:
The stickers didn't fit the dice (had to trim them)
The board is rather flimsy compared with Memoir'44 (but useable)
The game has units for Roman war machines but no scenario uses them and no rules for them either
The game only covers Rome vs Carthage - I want MORE ancients and MORE scenarios :)

RATING: 8.9
Great game using the Command and Colors mechanics.  I like this game a lot.  

This game is a two player game only.  You may be able to work out some variants with teams, but really I think it is best as a two person.

This is a great confrontational game, and one which can change quickly for the person with various sorts of advantages.  It is a VERY dynamic game.  I think of this as sparing or dueling.  I advise playing the same scenario more than once as different things can easily happen in them.  

This could be a "war game" but it has the feel of checkers/chess/othello also.  For me, it does'nt quite scrath the wargame itch but it does offer that nice feeling of conflict.  

The historical aspect of this game is limited and basically the battle fields are mentioned and title the scenarios but other than that, there isn't as much documentation/flavor as I would like, to tell you about the leaders, or their particular skills/tactics etc to really sink your teeth into the various settings etc.  Its a favorite as its quick, you do learn, and easy to teach/learn, but it doesnt fill my head once I have left that table.  That could be both good and bad.

RATING: 9
One of my ten favorite boardgames of all time!
This game is the perfect balance between simplicity and complexity.
The core system is insanely fun, but BattleCry and Memoir '44 were too simple. This game fixes that (and also adds a lot more actual tactics.)

The ancients theme was the one I was least excited about, but it's easily the best version of the C&C system (possible exception/tie with Napoleonics cuz I haven't played that one yet). Also, having just played through the entire campaign, I'm much more interested in the ancients theme(s) now.

Aesthetically I prefer green boards to brown (Memoir, BattleLore, etc.) but I LOVE the blocks. Admittedly BattleLore 2nd ed is the best looking of the C&C games (and it uses minis), but the blocks look REALLY good when they're set up ready for battle. Plus the symbols on the blocks makes it easy to remember any given unit's capabilities.

I just can't recommend this game highly enough!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.8
RULES: **** - The rules as written are quite a mess and leaves one scratching one's head in confusion, however, once you read a well-made summary, the simplicity of rules and, at the same time, the variety of possibility really shines through.
STRATEGY: **** - There are quite many strategic choices to be made. This myriad of options is usually whittled down to just a few by the Command cards you have, but it doesn't diminish the importance of the said choices.
TACTICS: ***** - The units are sufficiently different to warrant choosing, which ones are better to do the job at hand, and positioning is quite important.
LUCK: *** - Dice rolling abound, however, there are ways to manage your chances to some degree, thus it doesn't quite break the game. 
PLAYER INTERACTION: ***** - It certainly doesn't feel as a multiplayer solitaire, because each and every enemy move can and probably will force you to adjust your battle plan to make the most efficient use of your strategy card.
REPLAYABILITY: ***** - Many different scenarios, each of them offers different challenges to both commanders, making the game very unrepetitive.
THEME: ***** - Even though the rules are quite simple to grasp, they are sufficient to simulate the most general aspects of ancient warfare correctly, which is really all that is needed on the scale the game works.
BALANCE: **** - True, some of the scenarios you might play are not necessarily balanced, however, since the game is quite short in nature, this issue is quite easily solved by playing the same scenario once for each side and comparing with your opponent how you both did.
DOWNTIME: ***** - The game is quite fast flowing and does not devolve into long grandiose moves where one person moves his pieces for half an hour and the opponent has no choices but watch.
ACCESSIBILITY: **** - the game is quite simple, short and is for two players, thus finding opponents should be really not a problem. A possible turn off is war theme and messy rulebook, however, generally these are only minor issues.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Yea Gods, I avoided this game for a long time.  Why oh why?  Who knows.  I certainly played every other iteration of the Commands & Colors system.  Memoir never really hit the spot, being a little too limited and a little too simple (the electronic version on Steam is the definite way to go for that fix).  After playing more serious war games, C&C just seemed too simple and bland.  Oh how mistaken I was.

Two factors finally made me go for this one.  I got interested in ancient warfare after getting into Field Commander: Alexander (it doesn't hurt that Ashar loves the ancient world).  I also had the opportunity to play C&C: Napoleonics.  That was key, in that it showed me how much period flavor the system can have with just a few tweaks to the core rules.  Yes, this is still a fairly  light wargame, but it is plenty muscular, and certainly much more so than Memoir.

Games of this are quick.  Match play is de rigueur, and you can do both sides in 90 minutes.  The ability of units to "battle back" makes for a MUCH more realistic, fun, and speedy game when compared to Memoir.  The variety of Command cards really opens the game up; no longer will you be hemmed in by a crappy hand.  Leaders are great: thematic, and they work just like you hope they would.  The lack of major terrain issues is also a plus, and keeps things streamlined.

Really, the game is a masterpiece.  It went from being a "why buy?" to being my favorite war game to being in my top ten damn quick.  And the expansions look very tasty (although a bear in term of storage space).  I managed to snag the Greek wars expansion before it disappeared, and it adds even more flavor - not much in the way of new rules, but new battles that Ashar requested!  This is an excellent 2 player game.  Even if you are not into war games, if you play a lot of 2 player games, you owe it to yourself to pick this one up.  So much fun!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
I got pretty tired of hearing about the commands and colors schema being more suited to the ancients than it is to a WWII setting and didn't think that it was possible for this game to breath too much life into a system I felt I'd already become pretty proficient at.  

Boy was I wrong!

There are quite a number of ideas in C&C:A that make it a much different exxperience than in M'44.  Terrain is no longer the factor it was.  Instead, you really need to concentrate on the unit types and the ways they can be used to influence the outcome of the battle.  Battling back and evasion are of special note here.  They are tactics that aren't available in M'44 but that are ALWAYS on your mind while trying to rout your opponent.  

This game has made my heart race every time I've played it and I can't wait to try it again.  What a blast!

RATING: 5.5
[12/8/2008] Upon further plays, I've decided to bump up my former 5.5 rating to 6.5. I'm still very much dissatisfied with the lack of any rear vulnerabilities, but I've used a simple house rule adressing that in my more recent games. With it, I'd rate the game higher.

[6/11/2006] This doesn't really scratch my ancient battles itch. My main beef is that the importance of intact lines and the vulnerability of  flanks and a line's rear isn't really reflected in the game. A variant rule introducing 'facing' might do that trick without making the game significantly more complicated.

IMO, reflecting the above in the game would have been more worthwhile than the various unit chrome. Not that I dislike the latter or anything. I just think that the designer could have spent a little more time on getting the fundamentals of ancient warfare right at the expense of some of the details he focused on. As far as I'm concerned, the result would have been more true to ancient warfare, while cutting out some of the fiddliness of the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Like the other C&C games, I give this game high praise for being light, accessible, having a quick playtime/good pace, while retaining a good amount of strategy. I'm not too keen on the blocks - BL's banner system worked better for me for setup and distinguishing units at a glance (and stickering them all was a royal pain), but they're sturdy and tolerable. I love the Leaders and the evasion rules. Since every unit can battle back it makes things a little more heated than BL, though it does put less emphasis on keeping your units bold. However, due to the cards in the deck it's always good to keep your units aligned. Speaking of cards, the card mixup in this game is the best in the C&C series. Everything feels balanced and it feels like you get really useful cards a lot more frequently. My biggest issue with the game is the reference sheets. They are DEFINITELY not as user friendly/intuitive as the reference card system, and as a result it took me much longer to get acquainted with all the units in Ancients than it did in BL and Memoir. They're bulky, too. My rating for this would probably rise if I played it more, but dealing with the stupid reference sheets just puts me off.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.8
Does it feel like ancient warfare?  Yeah.  Just as Memoir *feels* like WWII.  Are either one a simulation?  No.  When you buy this, should you feel a little hosed?  Yes.  I would anyway.  Will I buy it anyway?  Maybe, but I would want to add some rules that aren't there and develop my own scenarios.  Basically change the game completely.  I don't really like games where to play them the way I feel they should be played, I basically have to reinvent the game (ala Polarity).  Is Memoir the better deal?  Without a doubt.  Only get this if ancient warfare interests you more than WWII.  That is an extremely small portion of the population.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Received via a math trade for Urban Sprawl.

Rated 9 after one play. I've played Memoir '44 a handful of times. I'm certainly no wargamer, but with Memoir '44, I appreciated the direct combat, the dice chucking, the maneuvering, and the streamlined set of rules. So, I was curious how this slightly more complex version of the same system worked.

Simply put, I like it better. Where M44 forces your attention toward terrain and position, C&C:A requires you to consider unit formation. The result is an engaging push and pull between two oppoosing lines as each player tries to find the weak link. 

Compared to M44, the complexity emerges as greater diversity of units, and also new rules such as evade and battle back. But more importantly, the leaders also add a fascinating dynamic. Units in proximity to leaders have a greater chance of "hits," and so effective use of leaders to augment areas of your army is something I'm curious to explore.

With one play, I'm very impressed, and I hope to play this some more.

Update: ...and though I really like this game, I ended up trading it away. For a brief moment, I owned M44, and this, and Battlelore 2nd edition. I decided to trade C&C: Ancients away. M44 is the easiest to teach and most approachable game in the C&C series, and I invested in Overlord maps. Battlelore provides the fantasy setting and incorporates setup into the actual gameplay. C&C: Ancients has the most complex ruleset, and I wasn't finding anyone in my local gaming group that was enthusiastic to play it. Off it went in a math trade!

RATING: 8
On one play. It has the feel of an ancient battle to it and plenty of unit variety. it is very playable. Yes, luck plays a significant role in it but no more than most Ancient wargames (DBM for instance) the command mechanism is acceptable and more interesting than a PIP system - though perhaps not as context related as Warmaster Ancients leadership rolls. Blocks vs figures? Well if you really wanted to you could play this game with stands of 10mm figures but the blocks give one the ready to run option are quite attractive and far more satisfying than counters. Improvements? Well, perhaps a zone of control sysatem (but one could argue the hex = the ZOC). Or more infectious negative morale system (eg. effect of friends retreating) although the postive effect of morale: supporting adjacent friends is there. Supplements? yes please - more armies, a campaign system. A fantasy version?

RATING: 5
Top notch components, in typical GMT style. As to the game itself, I found rolling straight to-hit-sides of d6 terribly luck-prone, especially considering the fact that usually you roll the same number of dice whether you have 1 or 4 blocks of a unit left. This is similar to Memoir 44, of course, and well established a mechanism, but for me not one I enjoy. There is quite some skill involved in learning to work your lines and managing your hand, and also figuring out what your units are best at. However, I never felt drawn into the storytelling aspect that each wargame ought to have, due to the very abstracted depiction of battles. This is a fine beer and pretzels game, just not one I will seek to play or own.

RATING: 9
Finally, the Command and Colors system gets everything exactly right, largely owing to the fact that the system is perfectly suited to Ancients combat.  I loved Memoir '44 intially, but repeated plays made me realize both that it didn't really feel like WWII and that it was really a pretty shallow game all around.   Aside from that I got really tired of the Euro-cutesy silliness of it all and when the overpriced and ridiculously packaged expansions came out, I sort of lost interest in it.  So finally, here is C&C: Ancients...it's got a much more mature sensibility all around and the added complexity in unit types and capabilities puts this in just about the perfect level of detail versus abstraction.   Momentum, elephant rampages, leader effects...in sum, this is the C&C game to buy- it's better than Battle Cry as well.  Hats off to GMT for a great production and here's looking forward to expansions...bring on the hoplites!

RATING: 8
I knew I would love this game and I do. It is similar to Memoir 44 and Battlecry. The cards have a better balance in my opinion. You usually never get a card that only moves one unit. However, my opponent seems to have an amazing amount of luck with the dice. We played six games and I did manage 2 victories. In one game I double timed with 4 heavy infantry and rolled 20 dice with a leader my odds of hitting were 55/120 which means I should have had 9.16 hits I got 6 and retreated two of his units. He attacks back with a leader and a separated unit odds of 23/54 with 9 dice he should get 3.8 hits he hits on all 9 dice. His archers attacking with 2 dice should hit 1 in three he hits double the odds not just in one game but in all 6 games. I saw attacks where his archers would wipe out complete units in two shots. I had one game where his light infantry and archers took out my entire center I mean they took out 4 complete units.

RATING: 10
[u]Very enjoyable[/u] card-driven tactical wargame and my favorite one! It feels like a tabletop miniature game focusing on the Punic wars (Rome vs. Carthage; although there are several other scenarios as well, like the Truceless War). The best title in the [i]Commands & Colors[/i] series because it emulates perfectly the ancient theme, it has an excellent deck of cards who rewards skillful play and it implements very well features like Evade, Retreat, Battleback or Leaders. Great components, playability, re-playability, simple rules but great depth. When I play it I feel like an ancient general.
Dice bought from [i]Valley Games[/i] (the original dice are terrible).
[i]My [color=red]Solitaire[/color] variant: for the "enemy" side, take only two cards. Select one and discard the other.[/i]
Mayday sleeved.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Was tempted to rate as an 8 and then upgrade after a few more plays if warranted, but upon reflection decided that it was worth the 9 rating straight up. This is the first true war game I have played and it was excellent. Loved the depth of strategy involved, it played in a good amount of time and has nice components. 

29/12/2012 - Played this one again (lost 5:7 to Tom). This one has really grown further for me so I've upgraded it again to 9.5. I expect it may get to 10 as I think it has real staying power. 

9/6/2013 - After 6 months, played this again twice (been playing too many other things) and really loved it. It hasn't lost any of its appeal and its rating is still justified. Lost the first scenario 2-6, but then managed to turn the course of history on the second scenario 7-4.

7/12/13 - Played the next two scenarios with Tom (was fighting against history each time). Won the first scenario 6-3 as Tom charged his warriors over the hill trying to score a quick victory. Fortunately my 2 command cards were sufficiently useful to counter and had some good rolls. This (and Tom's lack of use of cavalry and ranged units) put him on the back foot. The next scenario was relatively even to start however Tom started to make use of his greater range which took its toll on my line. This put him up on banners around midgame. There was then a clear turning point in the scenario where I managed to use double time to strike with 2 heavy units 1 medium (with 2 leaders) against Tom's line. Alas my rolls were terrible (scoring only one hit with 5 dice in one case) and then Tom's subsequent counter attack crushed me.

2/1/14 - Played the next three scenarios as Rome and was able to pull off a victory each time (preserving history twice and changing it once). One scenario was clearly in Rome's favour and was a relatively easy victory. The other two were very close (one banner margin each time), and I played some aggressive strategies that could have backfired, however fortune was with me both times. 

20/6/14 - Finished the base game campaign, with Rome (me) victorious 8 scenarios to 7. Going into the last two battles, Carthage was ahead 7 to 6, however Rome was able to preserve history and take the final two for the win. Really great game.

RATING: 8
Commands and Colors System:  the one where the use of leaders and formations make a huge difference.  Certain cards make use of line formations, which are useless if you haven't arranged your troops to take advantage of them.  Similarly, your placement of leaders will determine whether you can take advantage of certain cards, more so than in Napoleonics, for example.  A beautiful game in the C&C system that plays differently from its cousins.  It's intriguing and requires some good knowledge of the rules and cards to be successful.  Your ranged attacks are limited to slings and arrows (of outrageous Fortune), so your medium and heavy infantry are pretty important, as are the cav.

RATING: 6
It's a decent simple game, accessible to all ages.
After glueing stickers to the cubes for 3 hours, I was glad I did not buy the expansions and was a bit disappointed by the fact that this game has no historical depth or any type of fog of war mechanic, which I was sort of hoping for, considering I knew these were cubes.
For better or for worse, 2 unit armies, like chariots and elephants are easy kills, which maybe makes sense in a way, because you have to pretty much use them in edge cases and later in the game.
I find the edge of battle field mechanics to be anti-climatic.
But overall, I enjoyed rolling the dice and it was always a close game. In just a few games I have seen great upsets happening in what looked like easy fights, with generals dying and heavy infantry crumbling to weaker opponents, for me that's a good thing.

RATING: 10
C&C: Ancients is currently one of my favorite two player games and at the same time one of my favorite solo games. The commands & colors system really shines in this ancient setting. The small tweaks that are unique to this game (leaders, battle back, line cohesion) really help to immerse the players into the theme. The blocks work really well as playing pieces, the illustrations are nice and when set up the blocks do look like an army. The huge elephant blocks look really intimidating when facing small infantry blocks. The blocks are also really easy to store and move around, making them an all around winner. There is still one question I'm debating - is this better than Memoir'44? I'm taking a rain check on that one, come back later to find out.

RATING: 9
Just received - the components look great, although a mounted board would have been nice. I have just embarked on the sticker adventure - takes a while but they look great. Can't wait to play!

Update: 1/16/2008: Just played my first game, after having owned the game for a year! It is very good - more depth than the other C&C games that I have played (Memoir '44 and Battlecry) due to special combat rules (evade, battle back, bolster morale, leader) and the greater variety of units. A big bonus is that despite the added complexity, my wife enjoys playing it with me, and actually beat me in our first game! I like the quality of the components, other than the flimsy mapsheet, but since I also got Expansions #2 and #3, I can (and did) play on a fully-mounted board, which made the playing experience perfect. I do think the player aid charts are a bit cluttered and confusing, so I made my own version (which I will post at some point), and my own complete, expanded sequence of play sheet, both of which greatly aided our first play. I definitely recommend this game!

RATING: 9
My favorite of the C&C  series of games.  Why?  Everybody can battle back.  This helps mitigate not being able to activate units in a section that your opponent is making a thrust in.  I also like evade.  It took me awhile to understand when to use it, but it is a lifesaver.  I also like leaders.  They add a nice touch, but can and do die.  I also like the wide array of units.  At first it may seem confusing to keep them all straight, but there is a handy reference sheet included, and after a few games you seldom need it.  There are a bunch of scenarios in the box, and others to be found if you look.  The game is supported with several expansions.

I like the blocks.  They are easy to collectively pick up and move, more so than miniatures.  The dice are crap, but functional.  I ordered the nice ones from Valley Games.

RATING: 5
A light version of SPQR, this game has several things going for it - short playing time, relatively easy movement and combat system, a variety of unit types, and leadership bonuses.  However, there are couple sticking points.  The first is that damaged units fight as effectively as full strength units.  More problematic, however, is that tactical simulations just don't work right when card driven.  The flow is too chaotic, the command and control is goofy, and a few of the better cards can make or break the battle at crucial moments.  This combination more or less reduces the game to a card/dice luck fest, even if it doesn't feel like it at first.

I'd rather just play [i]SPQR[/i].

RATING: 9
Wow, what an exciting game!  Unit differences are tangible and can be put to effective use.  Unlike Memoir '44, the sparse terrain in C&CA enables you to react to your opponent's manuever rather than navigating a maze of obstacles.  Leaders and retreat rules are wonderful.  Scenario sides are asymmetric but balanced.  Tense and thematic, captures the feeling of command better than M44.  Impressive.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The game is great fun and a great step forward for the Borg games. It actually reflects the dynamics of ancient warfare pretty well. If the physical components were better I would rate the game higher, and I'm baffled that more people haven't commented on the lack of quality in the game components. This game retails for $15 more than Memoir 44, but delivers about half the physical quality. I think GMT spent more money on the box than they did on the map, which is the heavy cardstock they call "deluxe" in some of their other games. Instead of neat little miniatures, you get wooden blocks and sheets of stickers that you have to put on yourself. No plastic sorting tray like Memoir or Battle Cry, and the dice are simply awful (Valley Games makes a great replacement set, but, of course, this will cost you extra). All that said, once you get used to the blocks, they look pretty good, and as noted earlier, game play is very good. (They introduced a much nicer board with Roman Civil Wars expansion.) 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Category: Light, tactical wargame.

This is the first game I pull out when trying to get somebody involved in wargaming. Playing with an experienced opponent, you can pretty clearly understand the strategic considerations during your first play. The ease of rules and play enable clear focus around strategy and tactics without flipping thru rulebooks or pouring over charts. It is card-driven, so you need to be comfortable with giving up some control to the deck.

RATING: 9
This might just hit the sweet spot for me, in terms of complexity versus flexibility versus "realism".  Units are just different enough to have character; record-keeping is simple, yet gives a good picture of relative "fragility", card-order system isn't as limiting as I thought it might be.  Need more plays to refine some of these thoughts.

RATING: 8
I am a huge fan of ancient history and Rome holds a special place in my heart.  This is a game I have never played against another player.  It is one of the only games I have purchased knowing I might never have the proper partner to get it to the table.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Command & Colors: Ancients originally appealed to me because of its parallels with my board gaming history. Tactically, it is very similar to the CMG's I played in the past, and many of the lessons I learned with those games carried over to Command & Colors. Command & Colors: Ancients' advantage was its ease to learn. The other CMGs I played had both complicated rules and a need to learn the different abilities of each and every unit. With Command & Colors, only a handful of units needed to be learned before play, meaning that even those who had difficulty dealing with the large information that a CMG presented could play. These traits alone would not be enough to place Command & Colors: Ancients in my Top 10, however. 

While Command & Colors: Ancients has a number of expceptional rules that help make it great, what really makes it stand out among game is the hand management aspect. By providing a random set of cards to the player, they force the individual to build a plan based on the cards, providing immense replayability for any given scenario and testing entirely different skillsets then one would find if the cards were absent. Since I enjoy hand management in general, its presence in this game is a definite plus for me. The other rules simply add to the number of interesting decisions that a player has available, increasing the overall nuances of the game and creating a rich experience that is tough to replicate and almost impossible to beat in games of this class. The variety of scenarios provides even more replayability and fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I must admit that at first I was a bit disappointed that this felt about 90% similar to Mem 44. I knew the two were related, but I thought their would be more differences. I liked Mem 44, but my two least favorite aspects (silly retreat conditions and "trophy hunting" for victory) are still present here.

The components are a mixed bag. Honestly, I think I'd prefer large counters over the wood blocks, and strength markers for hits.  Fewer counters, easier to move and manipulate, lighter weight and less storage space required, and it would bring down the cost considerably. Still, I can see where the wood blocks, while totally cosmetic, will have a wider appeal for a non-wargaming audience. The map is also a mixed bag. While nice and functional, I'd have prefered paper to the carboard, which is slightly warped and doesn't lay flat, even after backfolding. Also, the way they split the cardboard map means it's more likely to rip along the seams than a paper map. Finally, the cardboard is heavier and bulkier, and drives up the production costs.

The cards are decent quality, though nothing special. The art is good, both on units and cards. My cards are not all cut the same- some have rounded corners, and others are lopped off along the right angles. Weird. Still, they shuffle well so it's a very minor gripe.

UPDATE: After spending more time with the game, I can certainly see it's appeal. I like it better than Mem 44, as I think the (nearly identical) rules are a better fit for ancient line-style warfare than a fluid WWII environment. I'm still not crazy about the combat system (ranged combat being the least believable- your chances of doing a hit to a light, medium, heavy, or cav unit are all exactly the same) but I can live with the sacrifices in realism for speed and playability. 

I've now played several ancients systems: GBOH, Simple GBOH, Ancients (3W), C&C: Ancients, and I'd say my very favorite is the Simple GBOH rules set.  For only a marginal increase in complexity, they feel much more realistic than C&C Ancients, and the fun factor is just higher. I also prefer activating by formation and trying to seize the turn rather than the force-you-hand card mechanics. However, there are lot of case where I'd opt to play C&C Ancients over Simple GBOH, especially if playing with someone who is new to wargames, or if time is an issue (although, honestly, many Simple GBOH battles aren't that much longer than the battles here).

Overall I'm pleased that I own this game, and I think games like this fill a nice niche in the hobby- for myself as well as the hobby in general.

UPDATE to UPDATE: As I play more and more, my rating continues to creep upward. This makes a great filler game, or for when time and/or concentration is an issue. And my wife will play it with me repeatedly, which is a real plus in my household.

RATING: 2.4
I was thinking of rating it a 3, but then I saw that 2 was defined as "extremely annoying".  I don't regard the cards as a fatal objection, or even the isolated significance of a few die rolls here or there.  Usually the fewer the  units, the bigger the role played by luck.  What annoys me extremely is that sound linear tactics are either unimportant or seriously flawed gameplay.  A coherent formation is not necessary; massing troops against a weakpoint can be very risky; contrary to some of the comments here, good units are not sufficiently better than weak units; and, most importantly, attacking the enemy's rear is actually suicidal. I'm not saying a gamer cannot develop effective tactics for this game, it is just that any knowledge of ancient warfare is likely to be a hindrance to his efforts.

RATING: 8.5
This is a great game.  I've played about 7-8 times now and I like it more and more.  Has a great feel, atmosphere, strategy.  Definetely not just a lucky rolls game.  Really it's the first war-like game I have ever played beyond chess or risk.  I love the cardplay, although definetely get deck protectors for these.  I'm fond of the wood blocks.  The board I would have preferred mounted, but with plexiglass it's OK.  The dice are awful, but I bought Doom dice, painted them black, color copies and trimmed the stickers, and then clear coated and they are real nice now.  I have mixed feelings about the expansions - I kind of like it and am excited, but I don't like the idea that I have to spend another $40-45 to get an expansion.  I'd like the game to be able to use the components I already have and support it more in terms of releasing expansions.  Perhaps I'm asking for generic bits and that would reduce the atmostphere.  But I'm not sure I want to sticker and pay for another 300+ blocks.  Anyway, great game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
An improvement over the original design of Memoir '44.  The added complexity adds decisions in battle and that is much appreciated.  I can see this engine yielding tons and tons of scenarios; striking a good playable balance will be interesting to explore.  An odd change that I think was actually a reduction from M'44 is the lack of objective control.  So much importance was attached to holding a key part of the battlefield, even in the ancient times, that I find it weird to remove that part of the game.  Especially because it tends to encourage situations where advantage is gained through mad dashes to reduced units.  Never mind how incohesive your line becomes or hoow dispersed your units are across the battlefield.  Strange but not a showstopper.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Not as fast and furious as Memoir '44, but definitely a meatier and more robust game.  I love the variety of tactical options, as well as  the importance of leaders and maintaining a line, the latter of which is especially well simulated.  Once you get the right command cards, you'll need spend a few turns setting up your forces to make one or two big moves. 

Bonus - my wife loves it, despite her dislike of Memoir.  The big differences were: 1) the theme, and 2) options on defense (Battle Back, Evade, etc).

EDIT: Received a set of Valley Games dice from Hawkeye77 for Christmas 2008!  Thank you!  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
 I'm glad I have this game and I love the wooden blocks.  I think my interest in it is hurt from playing lots of historical miniatures with loads of beautiful scenery.  The game play is fun and fast and the end can really swing with the cards and dice!  If it just had a more interesting game board it would make me want to set it up more often.  As good as the battles are, I'm left wishing the game had a small campaign system to flesh things out a bit and link the rewards of one battle to the next. Still, I'd recommend this game for it's great 'miniatures' feel.

Update: This game and it's Greek add-on has become my favorite light wargame. And 'light wargame' Is a hard niche for me to fill- I can't think of another game that is this fun and this fast.  It's a great game to play with eurogamers when you have a two player session.   I've been playing it on the Battlelore board just to have a mounted board.   It would rate higher but sometimes it feels like a battle is over before it got started.  At times I've felt like the setup took longer that the battle.

Final Thoughts: I'd rate it higher but it bugs me that this simple wargame can't adapt to three or four players.  Its short playtime  would make it a natural for multiplayer nights but the Command and Colors system (one hand of cards per side) doesn't scale up very well.  One guy holding the cards and telling a team mate what to do isn't really multi player.  Same for Battlelore.  I wish team mates could each have a hand of cards and some good decisions, like a miniatures game would do with split commands.

Update:  Ok, it's the only light wargame that I've found that I really like. I admit I'm picky about light wargames.  The complete system is really nice when you add up the variety of armies and scenarios.  The blocks, artwork and finally mounted boards push this simple game over the top.  Beautifully developed by GMT.  I only wish they included extra decks in the expansions, so you could jump in anywhere.

RATING: 8
Two player wargame lite. It's hex based for postioning, card driven for orders and dice rollin' for determining outcomes.

It sounds boring but is in fact a great game and there is a lot of fun to be had. Nice bit of historical background provided with each of the scenarios, which made me want to read more about the period.

Plays fairly rapidly once you get the rules down and there is enough variety in the units provided to allow for different tactics but not so much variety that you've constantly got your head stuck in the rule book - a nice balance.

RATING: 9
This is the top game in the C&C genre of games (that is, compared with Battlelore and M'44). It has all of the wonderful core mechanics of those games but adds enough extra mechanics to think about and that truly brings what is a brilliantly designed dice game into a level that could compete with any solidly constructed wargame. Somewhat frustrated by the number of rule layers and exceptions there are, but once you get passed that you have one of the more thoughtfully designed hex-and-counter games ever created.  Brilliant balance of strategy and tactics.

RATING: 7
This is my favorite of the Commands & Colors Series.  It may even become my first '10' ranked game in the future, but I need to get in more plays before I can give it that rating.  I like all the other games in the series, but I feel this one focuses on the elements of the system I like the most.  

I particularly like the way that units in melee always "battle back" unless they are eliminated or forced to retreat and how units generally retreat just as quickly as they advance which makes cavalry very fragile when on the defensive.

There are lots of other things about this game that I love (leaders, elephants, cards, etc.) but those two simple tweaks to the system just seem to make it play a lot more interestingly.

RATING: 9
The best game in the Command and Color series. The evolution of this system is fun to follow. It is not as quick playing as Battle Cry or Memoir '44 but the extra complexity makes the game more interesting.  I feel that the system works best with this type of warfare. The pace of carnage in a Memoir '44 scenario makes many games over before much a plan develops whereas in Ancients, without mech units or artillery every unit must put themselves in danger to cause damage and formation and leadership is key.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
This is another one of those games where I can't see where ANY of the gushing praise is coming from.

The game stops being historical after the initial setup. Unstoppable Heavy infantry end up hunting down scattered units to get that last victory banner while the rest of the armies just stand there. It is not that uncommon to have plenty of cards in your hand that are worthless. Retreat rules are lame.
Facing (which was enormously key in real battles) is not even dealt with.
Your unit composition, the initial setups and card draw leave you little 
room for meaningful decisions; most are essentially made for you.

So not only is it ahistorical, but it is not very good as a game.
Before you buy the hype, try a few games before committing to buy this.

RATING: 8.5
I have never played a block and hex wargame before but I have played miniatures wargames. I bought this because of the good reviews it was getting and my interest in the period.  Have now played four games and, although its subtleties are still emerging, already I think it's a great game. The card driven system produces some tough choices and the combination of leader effects and mechanics such as momentum advance, battle back and bonus close combats make for some really exciting game play. Highly recommended, but don't be suprised to find it's tough to win with the historically disadvantaged force.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Probably the most well known of the Command & Colours series of games. The command card play provides both "fog of war" and supply/logistics/command difficulty. The rules are very well written and laid out. The interaction of the many troop types is well balanced and gives a good feel of ancient warfare although this is more of a game than a simulation - but a very good game. Scenarios are plentiful and the game is supported by many online resourses. Components are excellent - I like the blocks - and can be pimped with miniatures and 3d terrain for a great look if you want that extra visual appeal.

RATING: 6
This game is amazing.  It gives you a quick game with randomness but the battlefield actually feels like it's flowing on the board.  It's one of those games where a good game with two good players will have points where you feel like you're headed out of the game and then some heroic maneuvers later the opponent has the same experience.

Could use a few more scenarios in the book, though, IMO.

Regrade:  I still find myself playing, still great light game, would recommend it to others in a second.  Even over its other sexier plastic Borg system brothers.  This is way deeper and more interesting than even battlelore with the leadership system.  It should get its due despite its non-euro gamer/ATer production.  I can't imagine someone liking Battlelore more than this game, but to each his own...

But for me, there are other games I'd rather be playing.  I guess I'm just at heart a heavier game player and wargamer.  I'll stick with 7.5 for now.

RATING: 10
Commands & Colors: Ancients is a masterpiece of game design.  It's also exactly the sort of tactical wargame I want to play at this point in my gaming life: a variable, dramatic game with meaty, subtle decisions without the burden of complex rules or a long play time.  I wouldn't have suspected that Borg's Commands and Colors game engine could produce a game of this depth.  Memoir '44 and Battle Cry were fun, lighthearted games.  But they didn't feel like consim games.  C&C:Ancients does feel like a consim.  It's a skillful game, a thought-provoking game, and a game that really does feel like an ancients battle!

The game is so good that I like to play a scenario four times in one sitting if we have time.  I prefer to increase the number of flags required for victory by one to give to give the battles a little more time to play out.

RATING: 8
I very much like this game. I have played and enjoyed all of Mr. Borg's published games using this system, but this is the first time (along with Battlelore) I felt that the mechanics weren't fighting the theme. Gives a good feel for ancient warfare, and the decisions I'm making as a commander seem to approximate what a leader in the field would have to consider. I especially like the way the leadership and support rules combine to encourge players to keep their units together; once you lose order in your line, the cost of ordering your units goes up in subtle ways, and you'll find your troops starting to retreat where they might otherwise stand fast and battle back. Terrific. Rating has climbed steadily through over a dozen playings; I genuinely love this game.

RATING: 9.5
The research I did before buying into this one was time well spent. It's exceeded my expectations. I'm amazed at how much tactical depth and simulation of real ancient battlefield situations it packs into its simple, elegant rules. Much is made of the randomness of card draw and dice rolls by the game's (few) detractors but these criticisms miss the point. It's all about good hand management, timing and using probablity to your favour, and consistently rewards careful planning and tactical play appropriate to the period. With its quick playtime, ancients theme, great replayability and high fun quotient this one is a real winner with me. Prefer face to face but the Vassal module is a neat alternative when I can't get it on the table. 

Updated October 2011: After many, many more plays through most of the published scenarios (many replayed multiple times) plus Scenario X, both on the table and Vassal, this is without a doubt my favourite game for regular play. Always up for a game, any time, anywhere.

RATING: 10
Just awesome. A fairly light game that can throw up some really fascinating strategic and tactical situations. Fried gold, as they say. RATING: 7.75
Initial rating of 7.25

My chronological Commands and Colors history:
Battle Cry then Mem 44 then BattleLore now CC Ancients.

I would have to say that even though I am a WWII goof and I also like fantasy combat, CC Ancients is the best of the bunch. Ancients warfare isn't even of all that much interest to me

Q: All of these games are the same core system, right?

A: Yes.

Q: OK, so how is it that you can like CC Ancients more than Mem 44 or BattleLore since they have a theme you much prefer?

A: Well, Mem 44 is just too light for me when it comes down to WWII. I came up playing much heavier games on this subject and I like more detail than Mem 44 provides. That and I just cant help feel that the game mechanics were forced to cover WWII, they just don't feel quite right for the time period.

For me, BattleLore is a really fun game (other than the crappy goblin rules) and the mechanics feel proper for the theme. However, when compared to CC Ancients BattleLore is "flashy" and can be a bit gimmicky and a bit more dependent upon luck.

Q: What is it exactly you like more about CC Ancients?

A: The mechanics feel perfect for this theme. Compared to other Commands and Colors games, the play is a bit more refined and focused which gives it a more "gritty" feel. I walk away feeling much more satisfied in terms of a good tactical fight instead of lamenting hot dice or who got their gimmick to work first.

RATING: 9
I've loved this game for a long time, finally got it.

First comment: wood. I love wooden components, even if they're little stickered wooden blocks. There's something about wood that makes the game so much more important and real.

Second comment: manuals and box. Beautiful. The box is probably the sturdiest box in my entire collection. The manuals, while a little dense at times, get the point across clearly.

Third comment: fun. Anything ancient has my attention, and just looking over the scenario list had me salivating five years ago. Who wouldn't be thrilled with the chance to replay Trebbia or Trasimene?

Two reasons this doesn't get a ten:

 - It doesn't take much effort to get custom dice printed these days. The stickered plastic dice feel prototype-ish.

 - The stickers. I have a love/hate relationship with them. I love the idea that I could get some blank blocks and do up a couple of custom armies and refight any ancient battle I want to. But after 2 hours of stickering my hands hurt!

All in all, my only regret is that I didn't get this several years ago.

RATING: N/A
2 Players
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Initial rating.  Seems overly complex for what it is.  

Components:

Representing a unit's hitpoints by individual blocks seems wasteful, and really increases bulk, setup time, and cost.  A columbia-style rotating block would create the same effect with a quarter of the materials, and I would even prefer a flippable counter to this.  Graphics on the individual foot units are tough to distinguish, resulting in a lot of board squinting as moves are planned.  Again, a single larger rotating block or large flippable counter would solve this problem.

Especially for a 3rd edition, the rulebook is poorly done.  Typos abound, and it lacks an index, or even a table of contents.

Gameplay:

Fine as a game, but it doesn't strike me as particularly realistic or historic (though I am certainly no expert in ancient warfare).  My least favorite aspect of the game is that the basic rules (victory points are only scored by eliminating opponents' units and leaders) and the C&C left-center-right line-warfare system really constrain the scenarios that can be played.  Essentially every scenario has to be two lines of fighters bashing each other.  No escape-for-victory or siege scenarios are possible, for example, because killing opposing units is the only thing that matters for a win.  So it's a line warfare game where killing opposing units is the only goal.  Ok.  I'll just have to be resigned to that and hope it accomplishes this rather specific goal very well.

There are a lot of unit types, many of which are very similar, and I'm not sure the variety adds enough to the gameplay to warrant the extra complexity.  It doesn't seem plausible that the various units are only there for historic realisticity, because there are many unrealistic things about the game.  For example:  Why do units attack at full strength even if they are almost eliminated?  What is this, Street Fighter II?

I'm not really seeing where all the hype for this one is coming from.  I'll stick with it for a while and see if my opinion improves.

Update: Ok, this game is growing on me.  Bumped up rating.

RATING: 9
Great game that seems to nail the ancient period!  Love it.  Even though you can see all of your opponents' pieces, it may not help depending on what cards you pull.
