RATING: 5
Below average: CDG Tactical - Ancient - Easy 

Ahhh,I'm so frustrated as I really love many aspects of the game but what pulls this down:
-1 block units role with full dice (yes I know it is morale related but still I can't get over it)
-Swing of luck just too much for me (I like excitement but it is just too frustrating for me) 
-No battle back; hey you wack me and if I have a card I wack back next turn...

Plus:
-Accessible
-Replay value
-Time
-Components
-Modular nature 
-Various unit types 
-Loads of scenario's

Minus:
1) Attacking units are able to damage / eliminate units without the possibility of battling back for the unit in question, even a 1 unit block can create havoc; hits in combination with a flag are deadly. Often too powerful imo. This is also the reason why I can't love Meoir '44. I desperately want to love it but I can't overcome this issue and I depirately hope C&C:Napoleonics has the right answer.

2) I know that a 1 unit block represents a unit that is close of breaking, however, the fact that a 1 unit block fights as a 4 unit block in battle is something I have not overcome. Worse, I got more annoyed by it over time.

3) The combinations of the card / dice mechanic is really clever, however, a combination of bad cards with a bad role can mean disaster in one round. 
I know luck evens out but frankyl spaeking this does not occur in playing one scenario. It happend too often that a well played single scenario was decided by dice / cards.
Maybe this is realistic but not for my tastes. I prefer a bit less chaos. Chaos can be fun but I get a feeling here it is sometimes too much, WAY too much;

4) I found decision making rather obvious. Managing Chaos is one thing, manging luck another but too often (not always) I felt it did hardly matter what I was doing as it went all right or all wrong (dice / card combo).

5) Map is too small for my tatstes

Easy set up and played with interesting choices and a lot of luck: cards + dice.
However, timing, risk management and flexibility have more influence on your fortunes than it might appear. LUCK meets SKILL and it is all about timing: 
-when to attack where
-which card to use when
-knowing when to take a risk and when not
-stay flexible all the time

Sold it.

RATING: 9
I think this is a great introductory game and a tonic for the jaded.  As in all the CC iterations (Battle Cry, Memoir 44, Battlelore), game play is very fast and usually intense.  I've played very few games that did not come down to a final turn with critical card play and dice rolls.  The beauty of this system is that it is easy enough to teach to a novice and they can do well with a little common sense play, but it is also interesting for a grognard, especially when time for a game is at a premium.  I think the main thing to bear in mind with all the CC games is that they really are games and not simulations.  However, that is not a fault and I really enjoy this game when I am looking for a competitive game that is lighter in rules content and time requirements.  The components are good, in particular the rulebook and the player aid card which is excellent.  The map and hex tiles are functional, but no more than that. Applying the stickers to the blocks is an exercise in tedium.  Frankly, I would rather pay $20 more to have that done by GMT.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.25
Finally got C+C:A to the table; why I waited so long I cannot say because this is a very good game.

C+C:A is Borg's Classical time period implementation of the ruleset from Battle Cry with GMT.  It is a highly thought of but minimalist game, with several sizes of double-stickered wooden blocks for the troop units (no fog of war there), combat dice and a deck of command cards to both allow and limit troop movements.

Other than the thin cardboard board this is a hefty game, with most of the box space occupied by wooden units. It's a classy but straightforward style that fits well with the generally classy games GMT puts out.

The play is simple enough even on a first read, and while there are certainly a couple of rules that sent me back to the rules midgame (e.g, Leader attacks/evasions) it was not especially hard to find what I needed and get back to the game.

The first scenario seems unbalanced in favor of the Syracusan forces, but the play was straightforward, simple enough to setup and get started playing, and the hardest part was identifying units.  Compared to a set of hundreds or even thousands of cardboard chits this is a mere quibble.

Because there is some luck involved with the command cards, there is a variable luck element beyond mere strategy.  The implementation of unit activations and change of player turns was fast and efficient, and the game flows nicely with little downtime or maintenance.

The scenarios are plentiful and varied, mostly covering the 2nd Punic War, and of course there are several expansions available for this game as well.

I liked the game- I can see why folks would go to Plano boxes for this, but other than learning to recognize the units better and backfolding the map before play I have nothing to complain of.

On a first solo play (1st scenario) I'm giving this smooth wargame with many Euro virtues a 7.25 with room to grow.  This games does everything it tries to do pretty darn well; it's no wonder this is top 25 material.

Now, why DID I wait so long to get this out?

RATING: 8
Utility note:  I originally had this (and [i]Greece & Eastern Kingdoms[/i]) in tackle boxes like you see in the image gallery, but once we actually started playing I bagged everything.  Bags are really much more convenient for storage, setup and breakdown. 

[microbadge=619]

(Upgraded with the [gameid=138441] & two sets of Valley Games' replacement wooden dice—[i]very nice.[/i]  Also threw in a bunch of red glass beads to act as unit activation markers.)*

[imageid=154488 small]

[size=18][b]*[/b][/size]

[size=7]Mounted board:  I was perfectly cool with the one that came with the game, as it felt appropriately thematic to have a "paper" map to go with the wooden blocks, but repeat plays highlighted the negatives—too easy to bump and move, and it wouldn't lay flat, causing terrain to slip around.  (A plexiglass sheet, while utilitarian, broke the "pre-battle strategy session in a sandy tent" feel for me, and I'm a pig for thematic immersion.)  The mounted board is much, much nicer.

Wooden dice:  The plastic dice that come with the game are just plain godawful.  For ancients warfare I want a [i]wood-paper-stone[/i] experience—not the [i]touched-a-bug[/i] sensation of Chinese polymers.  Curiously, one set of these has matte ink for all faces except the leader while the other set is apparently done up in "disco blaze" with sparkly ink for all unit faces.[/size]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I must admit I've never really understood the fascination some have with ancient warfare. OK, Alexander and Hannibal were cool and the variety of troop types is unmatched in any later era of history, but this is an age where battle tactics consisted of nothing more sophisticated than lining up and charging. The card-driven system captures this chaotic feel very well, but the game's objectives are quite artificial in that winning requires only the killing of a certain number of opposing units. So success consists of safeguarding your own depleted units where possible, and finishing off those of your opponent. This, together with the inevitably close finish inherent in a battle of attrition makes the ending feel quite arbitrary and unsatisfactory.

RATING: 9
Initially can be frustrating, until you work out how to effectively to mitigate the luck. The same is true of the different unit types; Light Cavalry, for example, are difficult to deploy effectively until you understand their capabilities (as a missile firing screen, they can be great - ask Crassus c. 53 BC for how historical that is). Once you see how things fit together, then it's a fun and at least vaguely historical-feeling game. It's not GBoH, but nor is it trying to be. The advantages are the quick play time and the reasonable balance on most of the scenarios (in the base game at least). Over time, and multiple plays, the better player will usually win, although individual scenarios can be swingy.
The scenario X rules available on the CCAncients web site are an interesting addition, which basically port DBA army lists onto the CCA system. Some interesting match ups can be made that way, which are usually pretty balanced (if you choose the right terrain for your army), adding to the longevity.
I'd like to try the expansions: the Roman Civil Wars are the period I'm most interested in historically, but I hear there may be balance problems there (which is excusable in a more detailed system, but in a play-oriented, light system like this is less than ideal. I don't mind historically one-sided battles, but I'd like them to still be fun for both sides).

Raised rating to a 9, because it's so quick and simple to teach, but still provides a fun and tense game experience. Might be a nice one to introduce people to wargames with.

PbeM suitability: quite high, with the caveats of the First Strike card and the occasional retreat or evade decision (although these are often trivial and can be assumed based on discussion in advance).

RATING: 9
Commands & Colors is physically attractive, though the quality of the some of the components is below Eurogame standards (albeit quite good for GMT) - the cardboard map is particularly unimpressive, the stickers tend to fall off the blocks and the dice are ugly. The rulebook is longer than I expected and it is not particularly easy to refer to important rules, but nevertheless the system is simple while managing to capture the essence of ancient battles - the concept of flanks and centre and related cards realistically potrays the overall pattern of battles, while the subtle morale/support rule beautifully simulates the danger of exposed flanks. Scenarios play quickly but offer some scope for tactical decision-making besides the obvious card management asppect. The element of chance is pitched just right, allowing a player who is behind always to have some hope of snatching victory.

RATING: 10
In my opinion the best of the Command & Colors system by far.  The additional unit types, the ability to battle back, the ability to evade, and the ability to command a line of troops add several more layers of strategy to this game.  The included player aid is superb and helps immensely in learning each unit's abilities.  However, the added depth may be looked upon as "rules-fiddlyness" by lighter gamers and non-gaming spouses.  My wife did not like the game as well as its lighter sibling Battle Cry.  I know that some miss the minis, but in my opinion the blocks worked out to be functionally superior.  I would have preferred a mounted board and better dice.  The board is useable, but I replaced the dice by putting the stickers on nice heavyweight blanks.

RATING: 10
The Combat Results Table is built into the dice! Brilliant! This game is definitely one of my favorites. I'm always in the mood to play. It's quick and easy to set-up. It manages to provide a nice level of detail/simulation without the usual corresponding increase in complexity. The player aid cards greatly facilitate the learning curve, although I've played so many times that I don't really need them anymore. The wooden block units and large hexagons dividing the map board give this game a nice tactile feel. As someone who has spent years pushing small cardboard chits around maps overlaid with hexagons measuring less than an inch across, the C&C game components are a pleasure to use. 
This game is not meant to be an accurate historical simulation, and some complain that luck plays too big a role. Although there is some truth to this, it is possible to develop tactics that make the best use of what Lady Luck decides to offer, and while it might not be the most accurate simulation of history, it's real fun to play.

RATING: 7
Tactical ancients game. Nifty components, including a ton of small wooden blocks, extremely sturdy box.

First impression: I wavered between giving the game an initial rating of 7 or 6. The game is obviously meant to be fast and "fun", but even knowing it wouldn't be the next SPQR, the lightness of the whole thing surprised (and slightly disappointed) me.

Now, this is based on only a few games, so perhaps I just have to recognize and accept the niche this game falls into: More beers & pretzels than a serious game of ancients battles.

Being one of the few people in the universe who has not played any of the game's predecessors (Battle Cry and M'44), I am astonished to hear that C&C:A is a markedly more complex game than those earlier games. If those games are considerably lighter than C&C:A, it cements my impression that they aren't for me.

Back to C&C:A : Still, a game which looks great and where you can slog through a battle in 30 minutes (less than an hour, anyway) has something good going for it. :-)

RATING: 7.5
I've liked each stage of the Colors and Commands system and it just gets better with each installment.

I like the fact that expansions are coming out as they will only add legs and more play options. One of the reasons I love Memoir is for the expansions (and the bits), one of the reasons I like Ancients over Memoir is for the match in theme vs. game play.

Are any of these games bad? Not for me; I do find the lack of a card incredibly frustrating when I'm practically peeing myself in trying to 'Maverick' it out of the deck, but that's the limiter in the game system...it's a break down in command and communication, it's a faultering of field leaders, it's a good thing and strikes me as a different way of modeling the sporadic action of "chit pull" games that limit and dictate movement opportunities.

RATING: 8
If Memoir '44 is the blended whisky of the C&C system, then Ancients is the 25 year old Macallan single malt. The Roman period feels like an infinitely better fit for the system, and owning both I can now definitely say I prefer the blocks to plastic minis. 

The additions to the rules regarding Leaders, Evasion, and Battling Back are welcome, despite how powerful the Battle Back option seems at first (rendering Elephants--which at first seem like unstoppable forces of nature--surprisingly difficult to use effectively). New players will find it a bit burdensome to learn and remember the subtle differences between many of the unit types. 

There are plenty of scenarios that ensure quite a bit of replayability. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

And, a cardboard play surface is NOT flimsy.

RATING: 8.8
"Each C&C game is played very differently. With Ancients, the overall firepower is weak (but nasty)and the core of battle is the close combat. Typically, everyone lines up nicely - then everything goes to hell - then the survivors line up for another go. In my opinion, it shows ancient tactics well.

Now Napoleonics is a horse of another colour. Here firepower is deadly and just charging in is a quick way to lose. Cavalry 'can' be decisive against weak and isolated units when launched into the attack at the right time. The game plays more as a chess game and it often takes longer due to players discarding poor cards and creating a good 'one-two punch' combination."

https://boardgamegeek.com/article/9315188#9315188

RATING: 9
I marched on the city of Arkagas with about 20 sessions of Memoir '44 to my credit. As a fan of the system, I wasn't sure whether I was in for a mild disappointment ("more of the same") or a fresh take on the familiar. The game quickly enveloped my skepticism and then waded into it with heavy infantry. I love it because despite the luck of the dice (and to some extend the luck of the draw), it rewards solid tactics. If you break formation in this game, you will likely pay for it. The differences between units gives you lots of strengths to play up and weaknesses to play down. The maneuvering satisfies without resorting to the "factor counting" prevalent in heavier wargames. I imagine I could play each scenario twice from each side without depleting the game's vast reserves of replayability, and that's 64 sessions out of the gate, before adding five expansions. I shouldn't gloss over the aesthetic appeal this game has for me either. The blocks evoke troops on the march and make the board easily scannable. Ancient Warfare just does it for me in a way WWII never will.

RATING: 6
I'm ambivalent about it.  It isn't sure whether it wants to pile on the details or if it wants to be a breezy, light game with an Ancients theme.  If it wants to be a game, then why aren't the scenarios much more tightly balanced?  If it wants to be a meaningful depiction of ancient warfare, then why isn't there more emphasis on the differences between armies? (which is easy enough to show - most miniatures systems manage such)  Still, it is fun in a beer and pretzels fashion and trumps the available competition on the market.

I have a longer review up on the Armchair general site.

Update:  I think the Greeks & Eastern Kingdoms expansion is an immense improvement which merits owning the original release.

RATING: 6
I am not a fan of the Command and Colors games. The card-driven order mechanic and die rolling are too luck-driven for my tastes, and there generally aren't enough unit types to keep me interested. C&C:Ancients relieves one of those problems for me by presenting many different unit types, all with specialized advantages and disadvantages. It is the development of all of the new unit types that makes this game capture the aura of ancient warfare. Of course the order mechanic will still leave in question whether or not you can play to your army's strengths. Because of the unit diversity and my love of the genre, I'll play this over Battle Cry or Memoir every time.


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
After half-a-dozen plays, I've come to feel that C&C:A, especially in the Epic form, is the cream of the C&C system games--with the caveat that I haven't played C&C:N or the revised Battle Cry yet. As much as I love Memoir and (for me, anyway) have played the hell out of it (30+ games), I'd now prefer Ancients if time permitted. My only practical gripe is with the rulebook, which should and could easily be a quarter shorter and should be "case numbered" (9.0, 9.1, etc.) as is nearly every other GMT game. Richard Borg "gets away" with a verbose, hard-to-reference rulebook because C&C:A is a great game and fundamentally easy. The book could just be so much better, and I'm toying with a rewrite for my own self if nothing else.

I would also now say that while C&C:A is a 9, Epic Ancients is a 10. It's, to my mind, exactly the way C&C:A should be played, even (especially?) with two players. Expansion #5 has shite card holders, but the Epic-specific deck is mighty handy.

RATING: 6
A skirmish wargame based on Rome vs Cartage. Some interesting ideas create more depth to the game compared to its sibling Memoir 44, but there are many things which don't work for me. Firstly the wooden blocks are tiny and hard to see upright. On a regular table top, you'd need to bring yourself down to eye-level with the pieces so its very hard to see what everything is at a glance. 

Also, since GMT uses printed stickers for the pieces, a few symbols would've been nice (e.g. maybe movement OR range OR close combat dice). With so many units, its hard to sometimes keep track of what everyone can do. Components quality was definitely poor, from board to the artwork to the dice.

The game itself is pretty similar to M44. There's a bit of tension as everyone races to do what they can given their command cards. The extra rules add a bit more strategic flavor to it, but ultimately its still a pretty random dicefest.

RATING: 9

I can understand these complaints.. however, I love the puzzle aspect wherein I'm granted a limited hand of maneuvers and it's up to me to make best work of a tricky situation. Couple this with the thrill of the dice roll and back and forth swing of combat outcomes means this is actually more of a casual engagement for me. One that I can sit back and watch an episode of events unfold with great intrigue, story and humour! Pity everyone else would rather avoid such an experience.

RATING: 6
After reading most of the rules for 90 minutes, my friend and I were ready to try the introductory battle.    We didn't have to read the rules about terrain for this simplified battle.

Most of the rules seemed intuitive.   There are only a few tricky things we had to look up while we played.    Even so, it was fatiguing to read the rules for 90 minutes.

It was fun.   Once we saw how the units move and attack, it was a pretty evenly matched battle.   The momentum on the board swung back and forth once or twice. 

It took 2 hours to play.   We both said we would play it again in the future.   If we do, it will be more fun, not having to read everything first.

I like the game more than a 6 rating would suggest.   I just don't anticipate playing this very often, or with many different people.

RATING: 7
This version of the basic movement and attack system seems to be a much better fit for its simulated time period than what was the case in Battle Cry. The tide of battle can still turn completely on a couple of rolls and victory go to whomever draws the right card in the endgame, but if you just look at the ranks advancing and breaking it feels like a fairly convincing representation of armies moving around the battlefields of antiquity. One possible exception: you can get into some gamey situations where, because of the victory conditions, each player needs to hunt down or snipe a single weakened unit and not much else matters. Scenarios haven't necessarily been designed for fairness, but you should be able to play back-to-back games in 2-3 hours.

RATING: 8
Nifty system for turning guys in sandals into hamburger. You really get a sense that with all your feints, your move-fire-moves, and evasions that you're really on some dry field with cracked feet, getting cut to bits. The system is a fascinating portrayal of ancient combat just after the phalanx: every single one of the five or six things I knpw about the Punic wars gets a clever rule, a special counter, or a cameo. As the guy I played with said, elephants are like hand grenades - you might want to watch what you do with those. 

Hint on the 2nd battle of Beneventus: HIDE SCIPIO. Don't bother with the veeps you get from going out the back. Just pull everyone together and make the Carthaginians come to you. Just like Publius Scipio would've done, if he'd been as smrt as I iz.

Had to dock the game a point because if I really wanted to spend half my life staring at beige I would've become a civil servant.

RATING: 8
My second-most played game, but I've grown tired of it.  I wish it had a stronger rule on facing for the sake of  historicity.  That moment when my cavalry attacked a chariot from the rear and ended up destroyed by its "battle back" was disheartening.  

Flanking: When an attack is launched from behind an enemy unit (the attacker is in a hex row nearer to his opponent's back row than the unit which is attacked), it receives two advantages.  First, the attacked unit may not battle back.  Second, after rolling, the attacker may convert any of his attack dice to a flag.

RATING: 8.6
Fun game. What it definitely is not is "Great Battles of History Light" -- notably the relative strength of missile fire (very high) and the lack of any unit facing breaks the "mood". This kind of spoils the theme for some of us.   

What it does is is quick playing, tension-creating and a quick fix for the ancients starved gamer. I won't give up on (Simple) GBoH anytime soon, but this is another game and I do think it better than, say, Memoir'44. Now, if only light cav weren't so vulnerable to retreat results and unable to chase down light foot... 

UPDATE: The above still holds but I must add that this has become one of my most played and enjoyed games ever! 

The variety of scenarios is great, especially with the first expansion (Greeks & Eastern countries). Also a wealth of home-made and official scenarios are available on the net. Simply a great game, and great value for money. 

To add some criticism as well, the second printing/edition of the game introduces an undocumented change to the "Mounted charge" card (now all heavy mounted units may move three hexes and attack), and an annoying misprint in the terrain effects listing. Also, they doubled the size of the player aid cards, but failed to include a terrain effects listing. Obviously an effect of the game selling out surprisingly fast, but sad to see anyhow. Also, the rules still need errata/clarifications. 

Well, hopefully there will be a third printing/deluxe edition down the road. Still a wonderful game, well worth the price!

RATING: 8
Command & Colors Ancients is a really good "wooden blocks" style wargame. The maps are typically unbalanced, but do a good job of re-creating the sense of chaos that would have existed on the ancient battlefield. Obviously there is a lot of luck to the game, and this sometimes makes it impossible for you to push an advantage to its logical conclusion. But this is true for your opponent, as well, leading to bluffing matches based on the positioning of your leaders and strongest units. One other thing I love about C&CA is the fact that you can get the full wargame experience in about an hour and then quickly set it back up and play again!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is a game I hesitated to buy. It caught my interest as being an for me interesting  combination of history and strategy. I'm a cautious buyer though and some negative comments about it being too random and dull put me off for a while. However planning to buy another board game at a local shop, which turned out to be out of stock, I decided to take the risk. I'm very happy I did! After several battles I'm now able to comment on some of the supposed cons of the game. 

Is the game random? Not particularly more than war in real life is. I think some war-gamers are living in a fantasy world about war being a calculated exercise of intellectuals. Unfortunately it's more often a bloody mess with a longer lists of "unknown unknowns" than knowns (I expect war-gamers to understand the reference). Your strategy will never the less make you more or less vulnerable when mayhem begins. A war-gamer that takes a war-game serious enough to expect it to be determined by strategy within rigid boarders of plausibilities should instead choose abstract strategy games like Go.

Sure this game can be frustrating when not getting the necessary cards and rolls seem to turn against you. So yes, at this stage I'm not giving C & C Ancient a perfect 10, since at rare times the combinations of cards and rolls gets a bit too quirky. Still I've not seen it affect a whole battle and when it has happened it's temporary and eventually balances out. 

What I really like:
- the impact of analysing units and their position before a game starts
- the clever and balanced mechanics of movements and characteristics of units
- while simulating quite complex affects of battle rules are easy to follow and logical
- the experience is more important than who's winning

All in all I want more C & C and have decided to order the combo expansion consisting of original expansion 2 and 3.

RATING: 5
I'd love what this game claims to be, but in reality it doesn't give you the feeling of commanding an ancient army. The victory condition requires terminating x units rather than winning the actual battle, so to win you're often better off abandoning your positions and chasing a dying enemy unit that is out of the battle. The command cards randomise what you can do (I can only command light units or mounted units or 3 units on the left - how realistic is that?). Units don't get any weaker when they're 3/4 terminated. The dice cause a huge luck factor, especially near the end of the battle when they can determine whether the loser gets 2 more victory banners or not in one die roll; which usually endd up determining the winner of the game if you take turns playing each side. The C&C system is just deeply flawed imo.

RATING: 8
Great "feel" wargame, with more emphasis on reproducing the feel of a battle than on simulating minute historical details--which I like very much.The playing time--under 1 hour--makes this game very playable.

The rules are heavier than those of your typical Euro game, with many special cases intended to better represent reality (e.g., the rules for Elephant Retreat/Rampage).  However, one or two games should allow one to grasp them quite well.

Apparently more strategic than its cousin Battlelore (thanks to the role of Leaders, for instance), and a tad more complex than Memoir'44--which I appreciate, as this makes for a deeper game.

C&C: Ancients has all the satisfaction of Memoir '44 with the additional complications of ancient war technology. Because a player's strength largely depends upon her positioning, the game feels like chess. Even though you use dice to battle, it seems like the decisions that you make absolutely determine the course of the game. I'm thrilled to have more of this game in my future.

RATING: 8.5
Previously owned a second edition. Had to sell it unplayed.
June 2017 picked up a used 4th edition.
+ Exp. 2/3,4,6 from Meeplemart 
+ blocks for Exp.1, spare base deck, Epic deck from Exp 5, extra mounted board from GMT
First couple of games left me cold. New players really need to be aware of the potential effects of the dice, lack of unit support, and the lethality of blocked retreats. My next couple games went much better knowing what to expect. This game is subtle. Once this is understood, the game has lots to offer.
With respect to people believing that everything is too random, I think they are misinterpreting the nature of what the cards and dice represent, as well as possibly having some difficulty sensing the odds. The following articles helped with my understanding and totally changed my outlook on the game play:
[url]http://www.c3iopscenter.com/documents/CCA_Odds.pdf[/url]
[url]https://boardgamegeek.com/thread/1607216/elegant-combat-restricted-command[/url]
The aesthetic of this game also really speaks to me. So much so that I just HAD to make a wooden board to play on.

RATING: 9.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
WAR!

March in lines and battle!

I was worried about the luck factor in the dice and command cards, but I am no longer.  Choosing the command cards to play, how you will move your troops, and the many options to weigh make this a very interesting game.  

The complexity is not too high, but it is enough that you feel like you are commanding an army and can form a plan to execute (and to adjust when conditions change).  Its a great balance to play a war game that lasts 60 - 90 mins and the game seems just plain fun...each turn feels like a tense decision.  The battle-back, retreat, and leaders are cool parts of the game.  There are many scenarios to play in the box, and with all of the various types of troops this game is as much or more war game then I could ever want.  Even playing the same scenario several times, it feels different every game.

Note: The three hours of assembly before playing the game is a big thumbs down.  I didn't mind too much, but there should be a warning on the box for those who buy a game with plans to learn and play it that evening.

RATING: 7
Definitely a welcome evolution of the Battle Cry system - the battles now actually look more like battles and there's  greater variation between the strengths and weaknesses of the various unit types.On the down side, there are only 10 scenarios and many of them are one-sided.  Although the game is short enough to make a re-match with swapped sides feasible, I feel that long-term replayabililty will suffer.   My desire to play a battle whose outcome is pretty much pre-determined is limited.

Anyone who disliked the role of luck in Battle Cry and Memoir 44 will probably not like C&C:A either but I imagine that it would not be hard to graft a slightly less chaotic command system onto the already rather good tactical resloution systems.

So, not bad and definitely better as a quick, one-hour wargame filler than Battle Cry but I'm not convinced that in its basic form it will be a lasting pleasure.

Perhaps the proposed expansions (starting with the Macedonians) will provide greater long-term staying power (plus better dice!) although I quail at the thought of applying stickers to another 300 blocks!

RATING: 3
Too luck to me with little space for strategy or tactic. Go for Bonaparte at Marengo (2 players) or Fredrich (multiplayer) instead.
DETAIL:
-You can't have a strategy before to see the Command cards you will receive.
-After you see the C. cards, you can think that you have a strategy, but only for 2-3 turns until you realize the new cards has nothing to do with that strategy (sections, type of troops, movement of the leaders, etc), and you need to change it. It's a chaos. At the end it is not important what kind or troops you have or where...you need to think only in those troops you can use because the CC...so, in fact you need to react  only, turn by turn and cross the fingers for good cards and good dice.
So, go for Memoir 44 instead, because in that game you don't need to memorize the features of each different troop (in any case useless in CC)

RATING: 9
This is what all the Commands and Colors games should be.  Rather than three or four different unit types, there are twelve, and they are differentiated in such a way that they seem to follow their historical roles.  That is, light infantry is adequate to harrass (and then run away), but heavy infantry is a killer when you finally get them in contact with the enemy.  Leaders are crucial, as well as keeping your lines in good order, as several of the cards depend on those factors to be used to their full potential.  I can't wait to play again.

Updated after three more plays; raised half a point to 9.  I love this game; it's fun and quick but also relatively meaty in decision-making and historical detail.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Every once in a while, a designer comes along with a game he designed that is, quite simply, brilliant. C&C: Ancients designer Richard Borg has done the near impossible; akin to a major league pitcher throwing a perfect game, Borg has designed the near ‘perfect’ wargame. 

The game provides infinite replay, given a vast card system and the variety of units with unique capabilities. More importantly, the game FEELS like ancient combat, with the field of battle swaying back and forth, flanks being turned and then, just as quickly, reforming. Ranged combat and tough close combat, attackers pursuing and defenders battling back, and of course, rampaging elephants. As in real war, some units hold tough, while others, inexplicably, simply melt away and retreat. With card play, one’s ‘perfect’ battle plan simply goes to the wayside within the first few turns. Nearly every game features tense play, with a deep level of strategy given such a straightforward, clean game system.

The game’s block system is superb, giving the impression of infantry, cavalry, and other units scattered over the plains, jostling and pushing their way forward. The game achieves a primary goal of good game design: bring the players to a level of ‘disbelief,’ where one is no longer pushing wooden blocks, but leading units of men into battle.

The rulebook is professionally done, the reference charts pack a lot of needed information, and playing pieces are first rate. The game flows well, is fun, and each battle can be completed within 2 hours. What more could one ask for?

RATING: 7
07/02/16 Played the Ticinus River scenario twice (each side once). Both games took about an hour each.  

Having played four other titles in the C&C series, it was pretty good. If I ranked them I called Memoir '44 my favorite, Battle Cry as a very close second, then C&C: Ancients, then lower, due to its complexity, C&C: Napoleonics, and finally Battle Lore, as I don't care for fantasy.  
- What I liked about the game was: The C&C series in general . The complexity of this one was a little more than I prefer, but not overwhelming.  From what little I saw of the rules and scenario booklets they appeared fine. 
- What I was not a fan of was: Using blocks when they aren't for fog of war purposes (I own Hammer of the Scots and Richard III and love them). I think this game would have been better if they'd used exchange counters (ala Hold the Line) to signify reduced units. I assume the cost of counters would be less than blocks. Plus the owner wouldn't have had to spend a great amount of time putting stickers on both sides of 345 blocks. (To say nothing of this game's many expansion sets!)
- What I thought was barely adequate:  the cards (rather bland), and the unmounted map. The dice were fine, but I think 8 should have been furnished (ala M'44 and Battle Cry) instead of the seven provided. The sticker art was so-so.   

I'd be happy to play the game again, but I'd never buy it.

RATING: 6
Really, played only two times, the first two scenarios. First, as an eurogamer mostly (ex-owner of Memoir '44 - traded because of the absurd amount of luck involved), I just can't understand the cost of it. I know is standard for a wargame, but come on, this thing is more expensive than Twilight Imperium!!!! WHY? The dice are the WORST components that I saw in a game in a long time. About the game itself. A luckfest, with some strategy (more than on M44). Most units are useless, at least in the first 2 scenarios. You kill with the heavies or mediums... run away with the lights. Not many options strategically speaking. I mean, you'll only have options if you get the right command cards. Otherwise, follow the only strategy possible. Some cards can just kill your strategy, like first strike. I was playing a game with a friend, lost miserably, and he said that it was because of my "flawed" strategy. The dialogue: "Why didn't you attack me there? It was the obvious strategy!" "Sorry, I couldn't attack you, I had the troops, you were weakened and all that, because I had NO CARDS TO DO SO!!!" That's terrible for me. And when he attacks you with 5 dice and gets 4 hits... Ok, any chance you have to win is over in the first strike... That's ridiculous.... I can stand some luck in the games, I love War of the Ring, for example. But hey, that's too much. 


Now shoot me.

RATING: 7
It's been said before, but the mechanics that started with Battle Cry finally find a home with C&C:A. When you see one side's army fragmenting into little bits, and the other's formed into neat lines and columns, you can tell who's winning.

On the downside, the battle back rules tend to occasionally kill the experience for me. Several wonky situations have come up because of them, including a 3-unit cavalry charge against an exposed flank of medium infantry... the cavalry (including 1 heavy) all get wiped out by this one lone unit.

I think we're going to houserule that any given unit can only battle back once during each opponent's turn to mitigate these situations.

Edit: sadly, BattleLore has replaced any desire I have to play C&C:A again. That doesn't make C&C:A a worse game, it just means BattleLore is a better one in my opinion.

RATING: 10
I actually got my copy of this game from Richard Borg, himself.  Was at Gencon 2006 and no one had it.  A friend and I were walking around the miniature hall when we saw "Richard Borg" on a name tag.  He's a fantastically nice guy, and we ended up talking with him for a good hour.  When I told him about being unable to find a copy of C&C he went out to his car and pulled two copies out of the trunk.

For me this game has the perfect balance between rule exceptions (for specific units) and rule generality, cautious advance and "daring" attacks, and overall game time.  It's very satisfying watching mounted troops slam into a flank and do serious damage.  It is also satisfying seeing supported foot soldiers produce the chaotic retreat of such an assault.  Then again, I've always enjoyed the history of the period in which this game is set.  Finally, this game plays really well solitaire or as a two-player.  It's everything that a miniatures-type game should be as far as I'm concerned.

RATING: 8
I finally got a chance to play the new Borg game today.  We played 3 games. I know very little about ancients but here's how our games went. The first scenario we played was Akragas 406BC took about 90 minutes with us referring to the rules many times.  The Syacusan's changed history and beat the Carthaginians'.  Next we played one of the bonus scenarios, Metaurus 207BC twice.  Both times the Romans wiped out the Carthagians in about 30 minutes matching the historical result which was the beginning of the end for Hannibal.  Overall a fun experience, slightly meatier the M'44 with leader bonuses and evade and defensive fire rules.  The blocks are fun and great stickers but sometimes a little small for old mens' eyes.  There is a big deck of command and specialty cards, some of which can be quite devasting.  The bottom line is keep a good line formation and keep your leaders handy yet safe for best results.

RATING: 10
More complex than Memoir 44 or Battle Cry but definitely has the same feel. RATING: 8.48

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The set up is a major pain for such a simple game, otherwise it's reasonably fun. I'm surprised by the fact that flanking attacks, or attacks from the rear have no real effect on combat, making position irrelevant (except that if you're attacking from behind you cannot retreat, but this hurts the attacker as much as the defender). Since there is no positional advantage, there is little tactics (once you've understood that you should form a line to get support). The only decision making comes from choosing the card you want to play, but that's pretty limited, so the best advice I can give is to get good dice.

I consider it a good introduction to wargaming for children (meaning boys) aged between 4 and 6 who cannot read. Then I'd move to simple card-driven games.

RATING: 6
The battle system from Memoir '44 applied to the ancient world. The cards are nearly identical but the line command cards are a nice - and thematic - addition. Gameplay is more complex and there are significantly more unit types. 

Component quality and artwork are virtually non-existent: The board is of the same thickness as in Afrikan tähti, and the dice are unbelievably crappy. Artwork on the terrain tiles is really bad, and the command cards are not as easy to recognize as in Memoir. The units are too tiny: I can't even see the pictures on the blocks, let alone distinguish them from one another. There's one thing they've done right, though: The cards are MtG-size, so if you are into using card protectors, they fit perfectly.

Wargamers probably dig this. Spending 60 EUR for crappy components and having to waste a week applying stickers to wooden blocks just isn't my idea of having a good time. Most of all, the gameplay just is not fun. There's just too much unnecessary complexity, and the components are ugly and unusable.

RATING: 9.5
I own "Memoir '44," so the rules and game play system aren't completely foreign to me, but there is definitely more complexity to this than to the WWII title. It's closer to a medium complexity rating than light-medium, depending on what players are accustomed to.

On the other hand, the game design seems better lent to ancient combat than WWII and therefore feels more accurate as a simulation. Once set up, playing the scenarios doesn't take much time. 

My only beef with the game is that the card draws (for commands) can be either restrictive or not applicable to a given situation on the board, which can be slightly frustrating. 

Received my copy via mail in February of 2008. Purchased Expansion Set #2, "Rome vs. the Barbarians" in December of 2010. Increased my rating from 8 to 9 after completing play of all the scenarios in the basic game. 

Similarly, after playing more of the scenarios from the "Barbarians" set, based on the balance between ease and complexity of play, components and how great the game looks, and the fun in playing, I raised my rating to '9.5'. The designer did a tremendous job of balancing ease of play with complexity, strategy, and luck, all into a quick-playing war game that also has very nice components. Plus it just looks beautiful laid out on a table so in this case I give Days of Wonder Company an extra half-notch for the aesthetic quality of its components--especially the mounted board for the "Barbarians" expansion. 

Allow a whole lot of time (2-3 hours?) for applying stickers to the hundreds of wooden blocks that come with the game.

RATING: 6
A fun system for beginners; attractive components and generally simple and logical rules. However I didn't really take to the card driven system and the frustrating limitations it imposes on players. With commands like 'activate 3 units on the left flank', all too often arbitrary situations would develop which saw a straggler or two left languishing at the rear of the battlefield.
I like limitations on players in wargames, and a measure of unpredictability reflecting the chaos of the battlefield, but I think something like a chit-pull system, as used in Richard Berg's Chariots of Fire (a much more complicated game than this), does a better job than C&C style cards.

RATING: 7
If you can accept the concepts and limitations of the system (i.e.; generalizations in the weapon systems, randomness through the card draw which limits what you are able to do, etc.), then this is a game to be enjoyed and appreciated. That sounds like a backhanded compliment but it really is not. As a game, it has a lot going for it. It is fun (always a major plus), can be played by two experienced wargamers in an hour or two and is easy to explain to a new gamer. The mountains of scenarios helps as well. I gave it a seven because it is a game that I do enjoy playing.

On the other hand, if you are a student of ancient warfare, you can appreciate GBoH because it clearly teaches you about the different army's weapons systems and how they would work (or not work) against an opposing army. If you are looking for this in C&C: Ancients, you are looking in the wrong place. Ultimately, it comes down to what you want in an ancients game.

Oct 2014: In early 2014, I agreed to a trade with a guy on BGG named Andy Glass, of Kentucky. I gleefully shipped my copies of this game and two expansions, complete with $20 worth of hard plastic storage cases for the pieces in exchange for some nice Euros I was looking to try (Fleet, Burxelles, Copycat).

Andy Glass disappeared, along with $150 worth of my games.

My only sad and bad experience trading or selling games on BGG or even eBay. I had failed to check his prior trades, as someone else HAD complained about something that went wrong.

As of Oct 2014, his account has been deleted. There is another Andy Glass of Kentucky that has a profile on the 'geek, but he has not logged in as of 2012, with no activity (shell account?).

Cavet emptor.

RATING: 9

This game plays quickly and is very engaging.

RATING: 9
I was amazed.

Thematically this hits both Memoir and Battlelore immediately off the board. I mean, these mechanics fit better in the ancient struggles. 

And don't get me started with that help sheet... It's just magnificent! There is everything you need except the evade. Only get to know where to find the things on the help sheet and youre 7/8 there!
It's clear, repetitive enough and informative.
Terrain could have had some help cards or something as in Memoir/Battlelore...
The map is a bit lame. It can't really be bended well. I hope to get better map one day ...or at least a glass on top of it.

Adding those stickers to 300+ blocks... I found it fun. =p
Can't wait to do same in the expansions..! I only wish that stickers were pre-attached to dice, it was harder to fit those in their small compartments. Art in units' stickers were REALLY beautiful by the way. 

All in all, this was a great experience. Playing time was about 40-60min/scenario and replayability is of course very high. The only drawback is the time-consuming setup time, which takes about 10-15min or something. A lot compared to the playing time (But it's still worth it.)

I'm just a bit confused about my actions. Don't know why I gave this 9 instead of 10? Well, a few more plays and I think I'll be more reasonable.

RATING: 7
I enjoy this game, but I'm not sold on the Commands & Colors system. I don't like that there are no ways to cycle cards or that it's fairly common for one player to simply get better draws from the shared command deck. If one player draws the cards than activate more units or give bonuses to attack, they'll have a huge advantage. I think I would prefer separate decks and a discard mechanic. For example, I prefer the card systems used in CC:E and Manoeuvre to this one. That said, I do enjoy the tactical nature of the game and the way it deals with different unit types.

RATING: 7
2 plays; Rating -8-
I've played quite a bit of Memoir '44 and BattleLore, so when there were just two of us at a recent game night, I jumped at the chance to try out this highly-regarded implementation of the system. I understand that GMT is not Days of Wonder and that they tend to put function over form, but I missed the lovely art of the more "mainstream" Memoir and BattleLore. My complaint extends slightly beyond the whining of a spoiled child of DoW and FFG, as the art of the units made it difficult to distinguish them. There were a few instances where I lost track of which type of unit I controlled or was fighting. Finally, I was glad my opponent had picked up the expansion with the mounted board because the paper map included with his copy of the base game would have been even more of a let down.

All that aside, the game play was great. With my previous experience in the series, I did not find anything revolutionary here, but some of the additional rules for battle-back, trample, etc., made for a bit of a deeper experience. I quite enjoyed it and would happily play again, but I don't think this will be replacing BattleLore in my collection anytime soon.

RATING: 8
Firstly, there is some high quality production with this game - primarily in the rulebook, reference sheets, and box itself.  The blocks are OK, wouldn't say they are abnormally good or anything, and applying the stickers?  Yeah, that sucks.

Getting past that, this is a very, very delightful block wargame that really picks up where M'44 left off.  There are a lot more options for command, and the scenarios are quite fun to play.  Units are mobile, lots of melee, 'battle back' retaliation and evading are good things to me.  Truly a game of positioning - utilizing the flanks, the correct units, and leader support.  I will be buying the army expansions for this game when available, and I will continue to play this whenever suggested.  It is a 2 player game by nature, and I won't fault the game for that, but because it is only for 2 players it won't get as much table time as I would like.  Excellent.  Pick it up, but don't buy it retail fer cryin out loud!  Get it online, where the price is much more reasonable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a two player war game, where each side gets a pre-set army made up of wooden blocks, and then each player takes their turn playing a card to command one or more of their army groups, then draws a new card. There's some substantial luck here in that the cards you draw determine which armies can act, and combat is dice based, although it's an interesting dice system, not normal dice but dice that has the color of the target printed on it, with increased hit chances depending on if a general is near them. That being said, the luck didn't bother me as in the case of the cards, you have a big hand, and can usually do something relevant. In the case of the dice, there's strategic mitigation if you position yourself right, and also, a unit still hits at 100% it's strength even if there's only a single block left in it, which really mitigates a lot of the die luck. I found the game to be extremely interesting in terms of tactics, and I hope to play this more, although it's rare I wind up playing in 2 player groups.

My opponent commented that the system was similar to Memoir 44, but more complicated and interesting as this game has more unit types. I don't plan to explore other games, since CC Ancients has so much content for it, and I'm thematically turned off by games that feature any kind of modern warfare.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:nostar: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::star::nostar: Raw enjoyment

I'm a great big nerd for the Total War-series of computer games, and this is the closest approximation of the real time Battles of those games I've found in board games. But it's a great game in its own right too! Just a perfect fit for me.

RATING: 6
My first plays left me slightly disappointed as I felt too much was driven by the cards and -- perhaps to a lesser extent -- the dice. A recent session has caused me to re-examine that view: it's light fun with quick play that requires a certain amount of thought; the thinking man's beer and pretzel's game, almost.

My two criticisms remain, however. Relative to the short play time, the set up seemed long, while (perhaps more importantly) relative to the game's simplicity, there seemed to be a lot for non-gamers (the people who I'd most want to play this with) to remember regarding the different types of units. Therefore it's not really the very simple gateway wargame it could have been.

Nevertheless, my recent experience of the game does leave me wanting to play other iterations of the Borg series, both simpler (Battle Cry) and slightly more complicated (C&C: Napoleonics). I can't see myself ever picking up the expansions, though.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Unit variation and a good card set make this a game where you'll be thinking about manoeuvring your units to outflank and break your opponent up. Keeping your troops together in line and adjacent to leaders with effective use of different unit types keeps you thinking. The hand management is vital - setting up a powerful combination for an attack that you'll be able to carry through with - more important than in Memoir 44.

After a couple of games the rules become very clear and you'll find yourself breezing through scenarios without referencing rules or reference sheets.

I'm extremely interested in the history here - there are some fantastic podcasts and documentaries on these battles and the strategies, tactics, unit makeups and leadership behind it all. I prefer games over simulations, and while I wouldn't want too many games this streamlined, what it lacks in minute detail it makes up for with pure gaming fun.

I thought initially this would be too similar to Memoir 44 for me to own both, but after playing both in the same week I see room for both in my collection. Good stuff and I actually quite like the blocks.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is a fantastic game and system.  I like everything about it from the blocks to the cards to the modules containing armies from different time periods.
The tactical level feels right without bogging things down by trying to be a full simulation of the time period.
I like the way the cards dictate the actions.  Good hand management is important but not vital, knowing the cards like some CDG's is not necessary so the tempo of the action stays high throughout the game.  Anything scenario based I like, and this system contains a nice supply of variety, granted not always balanced, but the switch sides mechanic nicely takes care of that and since the game plays quickly, playing each side of the scenario is not a problem and a fun aspect not seen in many tactical games.
Then there are elephants, how can you not like a game with fighting elephants?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Light, fast, and random.  I've been steamrolled as a result of poor dice rolls and unbalanced scenarios.  That, however, is not always a bad thing, after all this is warfare in the ancient period.  We struggled a bit at first but now the game plays fast and smooth.  I love the wooden blocks, they add to the ancient theme, and look great on the board.  The fog of war produced by the card driven commands is tense and you can always play a card from your hand.  Over time I suspect this rating to go up, a very solid game that is becoming an addiction and a fast favorite.

We really enjoy watching the ebb and flow of the front lines with the battle back and evasion rules.  Great history lessons, easy on the eyes, and we always look forward to the next game.

RATING: 7.9
I had lots of fun in the couple times I've played it.

Solo play is fun too, trying to optimize each play (minus the first strike cards) for either Romans or Carthaginians.

What I like: the moderate level of complexity (variable powers for different troop types) and the dice-and-card combat system which limits and focuses what you can do each turn.

What I dislike: the limited power of ranged weapons and that many scenarios are quite unbalanced (for historical accuracy).

I do wish there were more scenarios with unique objectives such as "capture and hold spot X" or "get your leader across the board and escape past your opponent before the sixth turn". Most scenarios are simply battles of attrition...which is still hella fun, if just slightly one-dimensional.

In short, I'm "done" with the vast majority of games of this type, I'm going back to "old school" "hex and chit" wargames with CRT's.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
C&C:A is the beer-and-pretzels game you take home to mother. It seems to have found the perfect balance of quick-and-lite plus deep-and-meaningful.
 
While this may not be the most immersive game in my collection, nor does it provide the most compelling narrative, by game's end it feels like a lot has happened -- and has happened fast enough to allow another session.
 
To play it well, C&C:A requires something of you. It takes a little bit of patience, guile, and huevos. The game plays like a prize fight, the two sides sizing each other up early on then striking hard when the opportunity presents itself. Afterward, there's none of that gaming-fatigue you might get after playing an epic. None of that why-did-I-feast-on-Taco-Bell feeling after a quick skirmish.
 
Yet, the game's strength is also its weakness: While its hand-management rewards methodical play, it is a stretch to imagine it serving as a wargame's command and communication mechanic.
 
That is, you're often dealt a bad hand, and card cycling to improve it is one of the great rewards of the game. But that feels completely out of place with the theme. It robs the game of a satisfying narrative. The lasting image is of ancient generals (or their gods) sitting at the card table, not on the field commanding vast armies.
 
Still, I like the game lots, enough that I'm handcrafting the game's components to better-match what I think warfare at this scale should look like. Two last points I'd like to make:
 
1) I think (and I hope) C&C:A will be further popularized with future generations. It's not difficult to imagine this game appearing in one of those Air Mall magazines, lavishly produced in pewter, or the map as the top of a wooden bookshelf chest (to hold game pieces, a la Chess).
 
2) Even if this isn't the highest rated game in my collection, if ever stuck on a deserted island with only one board game to play, C&C:A would be a strong consideration.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
C&C: Ancients: when men were men, and elephants stomped all over them.

The Punic, or Phoenician, culture survives today in fragments. It provided the basic alphabet which became Greek in one location, and Arabic in another. Some have likened the Phoenicians to the Vikings of the Mediterranean, as they traveled widely and had a strong impact on local cultures, but didn't leave much in the way of written records for the scholars who would later write history books. Alexander spelled the end of Phoenician culture in the Middle East, yet Carthage in Northern Africa remained a stronghold of Punic religion, language, and culture. C&C: Ancients, is primarily a system - a system recreating ancient warfare. In the base game, the first few scenarios pit Carthage against Syracuse. Carthage was trading all over Sicily between 500 and 300 BC, and settling some on the western end of the island. The Greeks had also sent settlers. Settlers from Corinth established Syracuse, which would later rival Athens herself as a power in the region, and there was often conflict between the Hellenes and Carthage. The rest of the scenarios in the base game depict the Second Punic War, in which Hannibal marched up through Spain, across the mountains, and down into Italy (unfortunately feasting on elephant meat part of the way). The scenarios also depict the brilliance of Publius Scipio the Younger, who came to be called Scipio Africanus after his victories in Africa - the one at Zama ultimatley defeating Hannibal. Even though the scenarios here (all of which are assymetrical battles by the way) mostly allow the players to re-live the Second Punic War, one quickly sees that Borg's system will be able to nicely accomodate other ancient battles with ease.

Some grognards may call Ancients (perhaps derisively) a "lite" wargame. Sure, it doesn't take 6 hours to play, and involve a thousand fiddly components. But for me, I don't want that level of complexity, and I don't have the luxury of the free time necessary for those kinds of games - though I suspect I would enjoy some of them. Ancients boasts a degree of simplicity not found in many wargames, although it is apparently more complex than Battle Cry and Memoir 44, two earlier productions which bear a family resemblance to Ancients. In philosophy and science, simplicity is always in a theory's favor. It is a factor if excellence, such as explanatory power, or predictive ability. I think simplicity is also a virtue in game design, and here it helps Ancients shine. The game can be learned quickly, and you aren't going to forget the rules after a few plays.

The game boasts a great balance between abstraction and concreteness, as well as the kind of blend between luck and planning which I seem to favor. Unit movement is controlled by cards, and battle is resolved by die roles. So chance enters in prominently - but it is a sort of balanced chance - balanced by the differences between units and the distribution of cards in the deck. The challenge then becomes, much as it did in genuine ancient warfare, the flexibility of the minds behind the opposing sides. Competent generalship is more than planning well, it is being able to adapt successfully to change, all the while under tremendous pressure. Playing Ancients gives me the feeling of being able to exercise just such a virtue - although I do not claim to do it well!

Each scenario attempts to recreate the conditions of a particular historical battle, and so one side usually has an advantage (just as in life). Players can each switch sides in a given session if they wish. Or, like me, you can keep playing the underdog until victorious, darn it! The game is certainly a lot of fun if you simply approach the scenarios with a relatively blank mind. However, if you are interested in history and allow the scenarios to engage your imagination with respect to the flow of events and the setting, Ancients is the kind of game that you will have dreams about - during your sleep, and even your waking hours. The game also inspires the "just one more game" syndrome; this is a hallmark of my favorite games. It's safe to say that I might rate this a tad lower if it were the exact same system but some different theme. I *really* enjoy the ancient military angle.

Now I've said a lot of nice things, yet there is one thing about C&C:A which some people find off-putting, and that is how chance works in the game. Because you rely on a card draw to move units in the three parts of the battlefield (right, center, left), it is possible that you may have no cards to move left-flank units. Well, that isn't strictly true, as most cards will allow you to move a single unit of your choice if you can't perform the operation on the card. More accurately, luck of the draw may not provide you with any *good* options for moving your left-flank units. For players who like a large degree of control, and want to be able to move each unit at their discretion, this can be frustrating. Columbia's block games might be preferable to such a player, as you draw a card indicating the number of units you can move - but you can move *any* units you like, anywhere on the battlefield. I do not think inability to do this is a flaw in C&C:A, precisely. It's simply a matter of mechanical preference that will impact certain players' enjoyment of the game.

Component-wise, the blocks initially take a loooong time to sticker, but a) you can use this as meditation time, and b) the result is worth it. The blocks provide an excellent feeling, and fit the ancient period well. The dice, I could do without. I can't keep them on the table, it seems, and I hope better dice become available. The cards are excellent - stiff and with clear type and crisp art. Saying the box is stiff would do an injustice to the box. The lovely, glossy box which houses Ancients is the mightiest game box I have ever met. I could stand on it, and my forebears had names like "Trond the Red Menace" and "Lars Axe-Hurler". I like the artistic design, much of it done by the same artists who worked on Battle Line. The board is okay, it's thin but solid cardboard. I would pay extra for a nice mounted board, however. I'm looking forward to the expansions for this game.

My only real desire, however, is that I knew more people who liked to play this. Oh, here are some rules which are important, but which many either forget or get wrong:

1. A defending unit can ignore one retreat flag if there are two or more adjacent units, and may also ignore one retreat flag if a leader is attached to the defending unit. The total possible number of flags a unit can ignore is two.
2. If a unit retreats, but is blocked and so cannot move, and loses one or more blocks as a result but is still adjacent to the attacking unit, the retreated unit may battle back!
3. If a unit has an attached leader and that unit makes a momentum advance, it may attack again (because it has a leader) - even if it could not normally do so.
4. Read the rules for making checks on hitting leaders and everything about elephants very carefully.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A very fast playing wargame.  Much faster to set up than Memoir 44 and Battle Cry due to the small geographical scale of the battles (because you're so "zoomed in", there isn't much terrain); however, the rules are far more complicated than either of its predecessors, enough to scare away any casual gamer and definitely move it out of the "Eurogame" category and into the "wargame" category.  That being said, the fast set up and play is a bonus offered by very few wargames, and the greater level of detail in the rules permits a far greater sense of realism at the expense of transparency (in other words, be ready to do a lot of flipping through the rule book)

RATING: 9
There isn't any element of this game that is particularly new, but the overall package is superbly thought out, with nothing superfluous and just enough special cases to make meaningful differences between the different units. I think this might be the ancients battlefield game I have always wanted to play -- all the fun of miniatures without the fiddly stuff. It may be too soon to call it a classic, but I suspect this is one of those games you'll be pulling out of the closet to play with your kids a decade or two from now. I withhold a 10 only because I haven't played a lot of ancients games, and there might be a better one.

Full marks for the bits, as well. Apart from a few typos, the rules are excellent, and the blocks make a fine display.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I've played Memoir'44, Battle Cry, and now Commands & Colors. I like C&C best, BC second, and M'44 third. This is so even though I generally enjoy WW2 era games more than all of the others.

Great Points:
Even though the dice and the card draw are randomizers that can have a significant impact, strategy--keeping units together in formation, keeping leaders close by, etc.--is far more important in C&C than in MM'44 or BC, where rushing a single unit forward to attack is frequently a viable strategy.

Downsides:
The unit-type symbol on the blocks is small, especially the difference between green circle and green circle-white circle can be difficult to differentiate at a distance.

The playing aid tables for unit types are great. The last-page Close Combat table is worthless and should've been replaced, in my opinion, with a detailed Sequence of Play for Ranged Fire and for Close Combat.

To help learn the game, I suggest playing a game or two without terrain, elephants, and by first removing all of the special-case cards. In latter games, you can then add these back in, one at a time.

Summary:
This game is fun, relatively quick, and has enough luck that you can play people who are less skilled than you and still make a game of it.

RATING: 8
Снова Борг... Да! Но эта игра не даёт мне ощущения дежавю. Всё те же сценарии, командные карты, отряды, цвета, но - античные тактики удачно ложатся на механику. Это не точечные маневренные уколы Мемуаров, здесь лидеры и построения определяют исход битвы. Интересно, несложно, но достаточно глубоко. За возможность поьбывать в шкуре героев античности игруля получает от меня очередной "мой выбор" для игры вдвоем. Оформление спорно. Во-первых часа 4 клеить на кубики обозначения войск, во-вторых очень специфическое оформление. Сначала необычно большие черные кубики, деревянные бруски войск, лаконичный дизайн карт, пустынная гамма игрового поля кажутся серьёзным минусом, потом - просто особенностью, к которой привыкаешь. RATING: 9
The best of the C&C series. Don't be like me and avoid it for years because the rulebook makes it look SO complicated. DON'T YOU BELIEVE IT! This game is basically the same, simple game you know from Battle Cry and Memoir 44. But instead of providing you with the basic game and then sticking the particulars of the units in an index, the rules cram everything in a WALL-OF-TEXT approach that looks intimidating and scary.

Do yourself a favor and gloss over the particulars of each unit when reading the rules. Then print out the cards provided by BGG users that provide that information.

RATING: 7
Memoir '44 was one of the first games I picked up.  It didn't take long for me to start to dislike the game because I want more control of my army (I did not care for the hand management aspect of the game).  

The main issue for me was the left, center, right sections of the map combined with the cards you need to move your army.  I would look at the board and create my plan.  Then I would look at my hand and see that I couldn't do what I wanted to do and had to adjust.  Sure I could look at my cards first and make a plan, but I prefer a game system where the cards help me to carry out my plan.

Based on reviews that I read CC:A does not seem as limited in this way.  My wife did not like the charts and my son did not like the theme.  While I thought the theme of this game was a better fit with the left, right, center the issue was still there for me and when combined with my families issues with the game it ended up being traded too.  

I found the system I like in Battles of Westeros....if I could only get to play it more often.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
It's a good light wargame, very simple core rules. You get a big epic feel that isn't in skirmish-scale games (like Heroscape or Summoner Wars). The tactical feel is also great - you can feel that the heavy infantry is really slow, and that the ranged attacks are weak and plinky. 

On the other hand the learning curve is pretty steep, mostly because there's about a dozen unit types (on each side) and you have to keep flipping back and forth through the reference sheet for their stats. Also the rules for elephants are all over the place, it's crazy. Overall, it's not bad but I feel that there are better places to get what I want out of a tactical/minis game.

RATING: 9
Brilliant, simple and historically faithful card-driven wargame from the 'Battle Cry' system. 'C&C:A' is the finest example of this type of game, where units roll a certain number of dice depending on their strength and have to roll symbols matching the units they are attacking in order to hit them. The use of blocks for units seems to fit very well with the ancient warfare theme and the tactical aspect to the decisions you make seem to be in keeping with tactics from that period in time. This is an extremely playable wargame which really should have a wide appeal.

RATING: 6
For my general opinion on this 'series' of games by Richard Borg, see my comments on "Battle Cry".

C&C Ancients seems to have some of the more interesting scenarios in this series, but also the thinnest production values.  In contrast to Battle Cry and Memoir '44, there are some wildly powerful cards in the deck.  The scenarios also seem to be more strongly balanced in favour of one of the sides (in comparison to the offerings in the other games), thus necessitating playing them twice (and switching sides) at a sitting.

Update: On reflection, I was probably too hard on these games with my initial ratings, so I've upped them a bit.  Even my initial rating and comments weren't meant to indicate that I didn't like them or wouldn't play them - I do enjoy them, and will happily play them from time to time - but none of them really grab me.

40 plays: almost finished the first expansion - one more scenario to go. Stays at 9.
50 plays: after somewhat of a hiatus I am now playing my way through the second expansion and remembering what a great game this is. Stays at 9, but I can see it going to 10.

RATING: 7
A rating of 7 is that default rating given to games I like but don't love (like most everyone else). The problem is I should love it and I do as a solo venture. But my 3 plays with a live opponent were less fun. I don't know why. I do think that I am not thinking strategically enough. I tend to prefer tactical games and playing with my gut. The luck aspects (dice and cards) fooled me into thinking that playing this way was appropriate. But that not only keeps one from playing well (duh), it probably keeps one from immersing themselves in the game. 

I will continue to give this one repeated chances as I am sure it is one I will love.




Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 1
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 1
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 2


Overall - 1

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Simply amazing. I really enjoy the card-based commands system and think it actually emulates (notice I didn't say "simulates") many of the command and control problems of ancient combat quite well with a minimum of overhead. The haters can hate, but until I get enough free time to try and sit down to try and learn Great Battles of History, this is satisfying my need for an ancients tactical game.

By the way, I would much, much rather play with wooden components than gaudy plastic minis that look like I'm 10 years old again playing with toy soldiers. Just my preference, and I'll admit it's a matter of taste, but I think GMT gets it. That said, I also much prefer wooden blocks over cheap-looking, dry, cardboard counters that make me feel like an old curmudgeon trying to pass the time before the next Civil War reenactment.

It's also turning me into a completist! Though, I wish they wouldn't make a new army set for every nationality... just more blocks to buy, sticker, and store. I guess they look a bit nicer and more varied when on the board, but it's gotten a bit expensive and storing them all plus setup (not to mention stickering) has been and continues to be a challenge. Given enough time, I'll someday do the whole Plano box system using the nice Geek File images to label where all the pieces go, but right now just finding the time to learn and play things is a challenge.

I've already made a P500 order for Napoleonics and can't wait to get that!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
First Impression:

Amazing wargame. The Command & Colors system finally shows its potential. Contrary to its' poor brothers (battle lore 1st ed. and memoir) this game has tough decisions, variable units that make both sense and an impact in actual gameplay and some amazing concepts around leaders and unit types that are implemented in the card management. Here every decision matters, positioning and manoeuvering are of utter importance and luck plays the role it should (randomizing the results it should - not deciding games). The standard advantages of the system also come in play (great flow of play + variable commander capabilities).

Excellent game.

RATING: 8
A good solid, albeit light, game.  I must confess that I generally prefer wargames with a strategic scale to those (like C&C) that are operational in scope.  But this game is simple and straightforward enough that I find it a fun, quick play -- without much (or at least minimizing) the fiddliness (facing, line of sight, etc) that usually gets in the way of my enjoying tactical games.  Yes, the card system sacrifices some realism for game play, but I think of C&C as a game and not an attempt at a full simulation.  So, in summary, this isn't necessarily the type of game that I like, but I do like this particular incarnation of the type.

RATING: 10

I am one of the block lovers as well, they give a great look and feel for the linear tactics of the period. They are also easier to move around the board than a clump of miniatures.
